[USER]
you are an expert start up entrepreneurial Mobile game studio leadership team and expert team of investors, deliver the best of breed initial pitch deck flow. this will align against the awarded top 50 pitch decks. 


[ASSISTANT]
Here is a best-of-breed initial pitch deck flow for a mobile game studio, built to match top investor decks in gaming and general tech.

# Assumptions
- Audience: venture investors and strategic partners evaluating a new or early mobile game studio.
- Stage: pre-seed through Series A with prototype or early alpha footage, no reliance on blockchain.
- Goal: secure a first institutional round. Target length 14–18 core slides plus appendix.

# Brief plan
- Tell a tight story: problem, why now, product, proof, economics, advantage, plan, ask.
- Put real evidence on every slide: metrics, dates, sources, working builds, short clips.
- Reserve an appendix for deeper data: cohorts, UA experiments, comp set, model inputs.

# Deliverable: slide-by-slide flow

1) **Cover**
   - Studio name, one-line promise, platform focus, founder names, contact.
   - Single striking in-engine image or short GIF thumbnail.

2) **The Opportunity in One Page**
   - What changes create a window for a new hit: platform shifts, distribution channels, genre gaps.
   - Size with restraint: TAM, then the precise reachable slice for your sub-genre and platform.

3) **Problem and Player Insight**
   - Specific friction or unmet desire for your target players, shown with 2 to 3 short quotes or clips.
   - Define the audience segment and play occasions you will win.

4) **The Game: 15-second Hook**
   - One sentence pitch, then a 15-second gameplay montage or storyboard frames.
   - State genre, camera, core interaction, session length, platform.

5) **Core Loop and Systems**
   - Clear loop diagram plus two secondary systems that deepen mastery.
   - Show how the loop generates retention and spend opportunities.

6) **Proof the Fun**
   - Closed test results or playtest quotes, heatmaps, watch-time on clips, tutorial completion rate.
   - If pre-playtest, show rapid prototype clips and expert validation from credible reviewers.

7) **Content and Live Ops Plan**
   - Day 1 content breadth, first 90 days cadence, seasonal plan.
   - One table: modes, drops, events, expected uplift targets.

8) **Monetisation Model**
   - Primary monetisation pillars: IAP categories, price bands, battle pass, ad strategy.
   - Guardrails against pay-to-win and how value is communicated to players.

9) **Early Metrics or Model**
   - If live tests exist: D1, D7, D30, ARPDAU, payer rate, early LTV curves by source.
   - If pre-live: show a transparent model with assumptions, benchmarks, and sensitivities.

10) **Go-to-Market**
    - Channels you can win on, creative angles, community plan, creator strategy.
    - CPI targets by source, first purchase payback target, referral or virality mechanics.

11) **Competitive Landscape**
    - The three closest comps and why you are differentiated in fun, distribution, or economics.
    - A simple matrix with one clear wedge you own.

12) **Technology and Production Approach**
    - Engine, backend, analytics, A/B framework, build pipeline, outsourcing stance.
    - Risks controlled: scalability, anti-cheat, compliance, store policies.

13) **Team**
    - Roles that de-risk delivery: production, design, engineering, UA, data.
    - 2-line proof per leader tied to shipped games or scaled live ops.

14) **Roadmap and Milestones**
    - Past proof points with dates, the next four gates with exit criteria and testable KPIs.

15) **Financial Plan Snapshot**
    - 24-month budget by function, hiring tranches, vendor line items.
    - Unit economics summary: target LTV by cohort, target payback, break-even install curve.

16) **Use of Funds**
    - Allocation tied directly to the milestones: content, UA runway, data stack, hires.

17) **Why We Win**
    - Your durable edge in two to three bullets: genre insight, distribution advantage, production machine, creator network.

18) **The Ask**
    - Round size, instrument, notable terms if any, timing, intros desired.

**Appendix (as needed)**
- Cohort tables, retention curves, LTV model inputs and sensitivities.
- UA experiment results and creatives.
- Detailed comp tear-downs.
- Live ops calendar and store mockups.
- Risk register with mitigations and owner per risk.

# Self-audit
- Investor fit: follows the canonical sequence used in top decks, trimmed for games specificity.  
- Evidence first: every claim is backed by data, clips, or dates in the slide notes or appendix.  
- Clarity: 18 slides max in the core, visuals over walls of text, British English, no em dashes.  
- Actionability: each slide has a clear objective that advances conviction on fun, economics, and team.  

If you want, I can convert this flow into a slide-ready page outline with speaker notes and a checklist of the exact assets to export from your build and analytics for each slide.

# Findings from the web
- a16z Games materials emphasise giving non-gaming investors fast context first, then the investment areas and team. In practice, that means a crisp “industry why” up front, not buried later. citeturn0search0turn0search8  
- Sequoia’s canonical template expects an explicit “Why now” plus the classic Problem, Solution, Market, Competition, Product, Business model, Team, Financials. Whatever the labels, those beats need to be covered clearly. citeturn2search1turn2search4  
- DocSend’s research finds investors spend roughly three and a half minutes on a pre-seed deck on average, and they score decks on 11 indicators, so you need tight slides, early proof, and a strong Team placement. citeturn2search3  
- Deconstructor of Fun’s guidance is stage-specific. Pre-seed focuses on team and plan to first playable, Seed on soft-launch readiness, Series A on scaling unit economics. Do not show the same proof across stages. citeturn1search13  
- Games-specialist funds provide concrete templates and checklists. The Games Fund publishes an example deck format for studios, and GDC’s VC panel outlines what excites investors and common red flags. citeturn1search20turn0search5  

# Critique of the proposed flow
What is strong
- Very clear product spine: 15-second hook, core loop, systems, monetisation, live ops. This lines up with games-specific expectations.  
- Sensible investor arc: economics, GTM, competitive wedge, roadmap, use of funds, ask.  
- Appendix allocation for cohorts, UA experiments, and LTV inputs aligns with how games investors review details.  

What needs adjustment to match top guidance
1) Add an explicit “Why now” slide early  
Sequoia and a16z expect a clear timing rationale, not implied inside “Opportunity.” Insert “Why now” as Slide 2 after the Cover, then “Opportunity.” citeturn2search1turn0search0

2) Move Team much earlier  
DocSend and multiple investor playbooks weight Team highly, especially pre-seed and seed. Move Team to just after Product or right after Why now for pre-seed, and keep a short version in the core with deeper bios in the appendix. citeturn2search3

3) Make the stage-fit explicit on the roadmap  
Label the next four gates with stage-appropriate exit criteria. Example: Pre-seed exit equals first playable and 5 external playtest sessions, Seed exit equals soft-launch in two geos with D1, D7 targets, Series A exit equals payback and LTV to CAC proof. This mirrors DoF guidance. citeturn1search13

4) Tighten to the investor time budget  
Where possible, compress “Content and live ops,” “Monetisation,” and “Early metrics or model” to a maximum of three slides combined in the core, and push detail to appendix. This respects the ~3.5 minute scan pattern. citeturn2search3

5) Call out distribution thesis explicitly  
For games, distribution is half the story. Add a one-page “Distribution advantage” ahead of GTM that states channel focus, creative angles, and community leverage, reflecting a16z’s request for context plus differentiated access. citeturn0search0

6) Align section names with investor mental models  
Keep your game-specific beats, but mirror the universal headings investors scan for: Problem, Solution, Why now, Market, Product, Business model, Competition, Team, Financials, Use of funds, Ask. This meets Sequoia-style expectations without losing games nuance. citeturn2search1

7) Surface red-flag mitigations  
Per GDC investor panels, preempt common worries with a small “Risks and mitigations” slide in the core, then a fuller risk register in the appendix. citeturn0search5

[USER]
you are an expert start up entrepreneurial Mobile game studio leadership team and expert team of investors, rebuild the flow with the detail of what and why per slide as a tutorial for building 

[ASSISTANT]
Here is a rebuilt, investor-ready deck flow with a slide-by-slide tutorial. Use it as a build guide with clear goals, inputs, and quality checks.

# Assumptions
- Audience: venture investors and strategic partners for a new mobile game studio.
- Stage: pre-seed to Series A.
- Target length: 14 to 18 core slides plus appendix.
- Goal: create conviction on fun, economics, distribution, and team.

# Brief plan
- Follow the universal investor arc while keeping strong games specificity.
- Each slide below states: Objective, What to show, Why it matters, Inputs and assets, Quality checks, Pitfalls.

# Deliverable: slide-by-slide build guide

1) **Cover**
Objective: set promise and context in 3 seconds.  
What to show: studio name, one-line promise, platform focus, founders, contact. One striking in-engine image or 5 to 10 second muted autoplay clip.  
Why it matters: frames the category and quality bar at a glance.  
Inputs and assets: logo, key art or gameplay clip, contact line.  
Quality checks: ultra-readable title, no buzzwords, file sizes optimised, legal cleared.  
Pitfalls: concept art that is not representative of gameplay.

2) **Why now**
Objective: show the timing edge.  
What to show: 3 timing drivers such as distribution shift, UA landscape change, genre fatigue or new monetisation mechanics. One compact chart or timeline.  
Why it matters: investors look for market inflections, not static markets.  
Inputs and assets: 2 to 3 data points with dates, a one-year timeline.  
Quality checks: each driver tied to a concrete outcome like lower CPI, higher watch-time, higher payer rate.  
Pitfalls: vague trends or decade-old data.

3) **Opportunity**
Objective: quantify realistic upside.  
What to show: TAM for mobile games, then the reachable sub-genre market and your initial geo or channel slice. One bar chart and a single sentence on reachability.  
Why it matters: rightsizes ambition to an executable plan.  
Inputs and assets: market numbers with sources and dates, peer benchmarks.  
Quality checks: SOM is defendable, not a flat percentage of TAM.  
Pitfalls: stacking markets without overlap checks.

4) **Problem and player insight**
Objective: prove you understand your player.  
What to show: top 2 player frictions and 2 desires, each paired with a clip or quote. Define your audience segment and play occasion.  
Why it matters: accurate insight predicts retention.  
Inputs and assets: playtest notes, community posts, short reels of reference behaviours.  
Quality checks: insights specific to your sub-genre, not generic fun statements.  
Pitfalls: personas with no behavioural proof.

5) **The game: 15-second hook**
Objective: communicate the core in one breath.  
What to show: one-sentence pitch, 15-second continuous gameplay montage, genre, camera, primary interaction, average session length, platform.  
Why it matters: this is where excitement starts or ends.  
Inputs and assets: capture from a working build, no cinematic cuts that hide inputs.  
Quality checks: clip shows actual control and feedback loop, HUD readable.  
Pitfalls: teaser trailers with no gameplay.

6) **Core loop and systems**
Objective: show how fun compounds into retention and spend.  
What to show: loop diagram with verbs, two deepening systems such as progression or events, one sentence on how they create mastery and economy sinks.  
Why it matters: loops generate the economics.  
Inputs and assets: loop schematic, economy sinks and sources list, sample screens.  
Quality checks: loop fits declared session length, systems are additive not bloating.  
Pitfalls: five or more systems before soft-launch.

7) **Distribution advantage**
Objective: prove access to players beyond spend.  
What to show: primary channels, creative angles, community and creator leverage, any exclusive placements. One small table: channel, cost target, expected scale.  
Why it matters: distribution is often the real moat.  
Inputs and assets: creator list, community plan, placement conversations.  
Quality checks: at least one non-paid growth lever with a testable plan.  
Pitfalls: assuming paid UA will always be available at modelled CPI.

8) **Monetisation model**
Objective: show value exchange and pricing logic.  
What to show: primary pillars like cosmetic IAPs, gacha or BP, price bands, early shop mockups, ad strategy if used. Add guardrails to avoid pay to win.  
Why it matters: trust and clarity drive payer rate.  
Inputs and assets: SKUs table, price ladders, sample bundles, UX wireframes.  
Quality checks: first purchase path is obvious, soft currency sinks sized to session cadence.  
Pitfalls: copying a top grossing store without matching your cadence or audience.

9) **Early metrics or model**
Objective: anchor on data, or show an honest model if pre-data.  
What to show: if tested, D1, D7, D30, ARPDAU, payer rate, first purchase time, by source. If pre-live, show a transparent LTV model with benchmarks and sensitivity ranges.  
Why it matters: investors underwrite to curves, not adjectives.  
Inputs and assets: cohort tables, source tags, model sheet.  
Quality checks: units and time bases labelled, cohorts not blended, sensitivity ±20 percent on key drivers.  
Pitfalls: cherry picking best cohorts only.

10) **Go to market**
Objective: show the first 180 days playbook.  
What to show: soft-launch plan with 2 test geos, creative testing framework, community beats, creator plan, referral mechanics, PR moments.  
Why it matters: speed to learning reduces capital needed.  
Inputs and assets: calendar, backlog of creatives, test matrices, Discord plan.  
Quality checks: each test has a decision rule and owner.  
Pitfalls: long launch with no hard gates or stop rules.

11) **Competitive landscape**
Objective: position clearly against real substitutes.  
What to show: 3 to 5 nearest comps, your wedge in fun, distribution or economics. One matrix with one dimension per axis that actually varies.  
Why it matters: investors scan for crowdedness and differentiation.  
Inputs and assets: comp feature grid, revenue ranks, review snippets.  
Quality checks: your wedge is something players feel in the loop, not only a brand statement.  
Pitfalls: giant 9 by 9 grids with everyone in the centre.

12) **Team**
Objective: de-risk execution with relevant wins.  
What to show: 4 to 6 core leaders, each with 2 lines that tie to shipped titles or scaled live ops relevant to your plan. Advisory board only if hands on.  
Why it matters: pre-seed and seed are team bets.  
Inputs and assets: bios, shipped games list, role coverage map.  
Quality checks: roles map to the loop, economy, live ops, data, and UA.  
Pitfalls: long biographies or celebrity advisors with no time allocation.

13) **Technology and production approach**
Objective: show you can ship and operate reliably.  
What to show: engine, backend, build pipeline, analytics, experiment framework, outsourcing stance, compliance.  
Why it matters: reduces technical and schedule risk.  
Inputs and assets: stack diagram, CI screenshot, SDK list, live-ops tooling.  
Quality checks: clear ownership, bus factor addressed, cost to scale estimated.  
Pitfalls: over-engineering before product-market fit.

14) **Roadmap and stage-gated milestones**
Objective: align capital to outcomes.  
What to show: past proof points with dates, next four gates with exit criteria, KPIs and test plans. Examples: First Playable, Closed Alpha, Soft-launch 1 and 2, Global Launch Candidate.  
Why it matters: investors commit to gates, not hopes.  
Inputs and assets: milestone plan, KPI targets, risk owners.  
Quality checks: each gate has a measurable KPI like D1, D7, FTUE completion, payback.  
Pitfalls: vague milestones like polish or improve retention.

15) **Financial plan snapshot**
Objective: present burn and runway tied to plan.  
What to show: 24-month budget by function, hiring tranches, third parties, infra, UA runway, cash burn, runway months. Unit economics summary like target LTV by cohort and payback.  
Why it matters: validates ask size and survivability.  
Inputs and assets: headcount plan, vendor quotes, UA assumptions.  
Quality checks: month by month burn reconciles, hiring matches milestones, UA spend only when tests pass.  
Pitfalls: flat headcount lines or UA before retention gates clear.

16) **Use of funds**
Objective: show how capital becomes value.  
What to show: allocation by milestone with percentages and pounds, tied directly to gates such as content, live ops tool, UA runway, priority hires.  
Why it matters: converts money to progress in the reader’s mind.  
Inputs and assets: budget roll-up, hiring plan, vendor plans.  
Quality checks: totals reconcile to round size, buffer for overruns included.  
Pitfalls: generic buckets like growth or operations with no tie to gates.

17) **Risks and mitigations**
Objective: build trust by naming the dragons.  
What to show: top 5 risks across product, UA, tech, compliance, team. One line mitigation and owner each.  
Why it matters: shows maturity and speeds diligence.  
Inputs and assets: risk register, owner list, contingency costs.  
Quality checks: risks are specific and testable, not business clichés.  
Pitfalls: hiding risks that will be obvious in diligence.

18) **The ask**
Objective: make it easy to say yes.  
What to show: round size, instrument, timing, lead or committed if any, use of proceeds link, data room link, contact.  
Why it matters: closes the loop and sets the next step.  
Inputs and assets: term targets, timeline, data room.  
Quality checks: consistent with slides 15 and 16, contact line visible.  
Pitfalls: conditional language or moving targets.

**Appendix**
Objective: give depth without bloating the core.  
What to show: cohort tables, retention curves, LTV model with sensitivity, UA experiment logs and creatives, comp tear-downs, live ops calendar, risk register detail, hiring grid with salary bands, tech architecture pages, legal and compliance notes.  
Why it matters: enables diligence in one pass.  
Inputs and assets: spreadsheets, dashboards, Miro or Figma exports.  
Quality checks: pagination, consistent units, dates on every chart.  
Pitfalls: dumping raw exports without labels.

# Build checklist by role
- CEO or Studio Head: own slides 2, 3, 14, 18.  
- CPO or Game Director: own 5, 6, 8, 10.  
- CTO or Tech Lead: own 13.  
- CFO or Ops: own 15, 16.  
- UA or Growth: own 7, 9, 10, 11.  
- Producer: integration, timelines, polish.

# Visual and pacing standards
- 1 message per slide, 3 to 5 bullet lines, 1 graphic max, readable from 2 metres.  
- Video: 15 seconds for hook, 30 seconds total across deck, gameplay only.  
- Charts: label units and dates, keep axes zero-based unless noted, avoid pie charts.  
- Time budget: investors scan a deck in about 3 to 4 minutes, so load critical proof by slide 9.

# Self-audit
Coverage: includes universal investor beats and games-specific proof.  
Clarity: each slide pairs a single objective with concrete inputs and checks.  
Actionability: every element is tied to a test, metric, or milestone.  
Consistency: no reliance on hype, clean British English, no em dashes.

[USER]
should we add guidance for the quality and visual fidelity of slides?

[ASSISTANT]
Yes. Here is a concise, implementable guide you can paste into your deck brief so every slide hits a consistent visual bar.

# Assumptions
- Investor audience viewing on laptops first, boardroom screens second.
- 16:9 slides, exported to PDF for sharing, with a live presentation copy retained.

# Brief plan
- Define non-negotiable visual standards.
- Specify asset specs for gameplay, charts, and text.
- Add a QA checklist and an acceptance rubric so reviewers can score slides quickly.

# Deliverable: Quality and visual fidelity guide

## 1) Foundations
- Canvas: 16:9 at 1920×1080. Safe margins: 40 px all sides. Use a 12-column grid or a simple 4-column grid with 24 px gutters.
- Palette: 1 primary, 1 secondary, 1 highlight, 2 neutrals. Limit categories in any one chart to 6. Reserve highlight for the single idea on the slide.
- Type: one sans-serif family only. Title ≥ 40 pt, headings ≥ 28 pt, body ≥ 20 pt, labels ≥ 16 pt. Line spacing 1.15 to 1.3. Avoid all caps for paragraphs.
- Contrast: meet WCAG AA. Aim for a contrast ratio of 4.5:1 or higher on text.
- Density: one message per slide, 3 to 5 short lines, 1 graphic max.

## 2) Imagery and video
- Gameplay: capture from a working build. Resolution 1080p. Show input and feedback, not cinematics. Trim to 15 seconds for the hook slide and keep total video under 30 seconds across the deck.
- Compression: H.264 MP4, under 15 MB per clip. Static images at 150–200 DPI, PNG for UI, JPEG for full-bleed backgrounds under 400 KB each.
- Framing: include HUD when it communicates mechanics. Avoid tilt and dutch angles unless part of the game camera.

## 3) Charts and tables
- Prefer bars, lines, waterfalls, cohort curves, and funnels. Avoid pies.
- Axes start at zero unless a clipped axis is explicitly marked. Always label units, currency, and dates.
- Annotate insight directly on the chart in 1 short line. Keep gridlines faint. No 3D.
- Tables: max 6 rows by 4 columns in the core deck. Larger tables go to the appendix.

## 4) Slide patterns by purpose
- Cover: single image or short gameplay loop. Title and one-line promise. Founder names small at bottom.
- The game hook: 15-second clip, one-sentence pitch, 3 tags only (genre, camera, session length).
- Core loop: simple loop diagram with 3 to 5 verbs, plus two callouts for systems that deepen mastery.
- Market and why now: one chart each, one callout each. Do not stack more than 2 charts on a single slide.
- Monetisation: store mockup plus a tidy SKU ladder. One callout states the first purchase path.
- Metrics: one lead chart (D1, D7, D30 or payback) with a small table of supporting numbers.
- Team: 4 to 6 tiles, each with a title, shipped game, and a single outcome line.
- Risks: 5 rows only, each with an owner and mitigation.

## 5) Animation and motion
- Keep to simple appear or fade animations. Duration 200 to 300 ms. Max 2 animations per slide. No fly-ins or spins.
- Use builds to reveal complex charts stepwise during live talks, but export the final static state to PDF.

## 6) Accessibility and readability
- Colour-only distinctions must also have labels or patterns.
- Never put text directly over busy imagery. Use a 70 to 85 percent black or white overlay behind text when needed.
- Provide concise alt text in speaker notes for key visuals when the PDF may be reviewed with assistive tech.

## 7) Brand and UI discipline
- Consistent placement: titles top left, source line bottom right, slide number bottom centre.
- Consistent iconography policy: avoid decorative icons. Use short labels instead.
- Do not mix rounded and sharp UI shapes on the same slide unless it mirrors in-game UI.

## 8) File hygiene
- Master template only. Define title, content, section divider, and appendix masters before build.
- Versioning: DeckName_vMajor.Minor_Date. Keep a change log in the appendix first page.
- Exports: PDF ≤ 12 MB. Ensure embedded fonts. Provide a link to the live presentation copy for meetings.

## 9) QA checklist per slide
- Message: one clear takeaway, stated in the title.
- Evidence: at least one dated source, metric, or real capture that supports the takeaway.
- Readability: body text ≥ 20 pt, adequate line spacing, clean margins.
- Visuals: no more than 1 primary graphic. Palette adheres to the 5-colour limit.
- Data hygiene: units and time bases labelled, axes correct, sources included in 10 pt at bottom right.
- Consistency: aligns with template grid, title position, footer, and numbering.

## 10) Acceptance rubric (0 to 5)
- 5: Investor-ready. Clear message, clean visual hierarchy, accurate data, flawless spacing, strong evidence.
- 4: Minor tweaks only. One spacing or sizing issue, or a small labelling fix.
- 3: Needs revision. Clutter, weak contrast, or unclear message.
- 2: Major issues. Mixed fonts, poor data labelling, busy layout, unclear evidence.
- 1: Unusable. Violates core standards, unreadable, or misleading charting.

# Self-audit
- Standards are concrete and testable, with clear numbers for type sizes, margins, media, and compression.
- Guidance aligns with investor scanning behaviour and keeps gameplay front and centre.
- The checklist and rubric enable quick review and consistent sign-off across contributors.

is thier guidance on number of slides in the pitch deck?

[ASSISTANT]
Yes. Short answer: keep the core deck to about 10–15 slides for seed, with a tight narrative, and push detail to an appendix or data room. Investors typically skim decks in under four minutes, so brevity matters. citeturn0search7turn0search9turn0search0

# Practical guidance
- **Pre-seed/Seed, live presentation:** 10–15 core slides. Matches YC’s guidance and fits investor attention spans seen in DocSend studies. citeturn0search7turn0search9  
- **Seed, send-ahead PDF:** 12–18 core slides max, with an appendix for cohorts, GTM tests, and model inputs. DocSend’s data shows readers spend roughly 3 minutes 20–44 seconds per deck, so keep the story front-loaded. citeturn0search9turn0search0  
- **Series A:** similar 10–15 for the live narrative, plus a beefier appendix. YC still recommends 10–15 for the core deck at Series A. citeturn0search7  
- **Classic rule of thumb:** the 10/20/30 rule. Ten slides, twenty minutes, 30-point minimum font. Useful as a discipline check even if you run a few slides over. citeturn1search0turn1search2

# Working rule to adopt
- **Policy:** Core deck ≤ 15 slides for live talks, ≤ 18 for send-ahead. Anything deeper goes to the appendix. Use the core to earn the right to the appendix. This aligns with YC’s “10–15” and DocSend’s scan-time evidence. citeturn0search7turn0search9

you are an expert start up entrepreneurial Mobile game studio leadership team and expert team of investors, rebuild the full guide, compile all of this feedback into a clear guidance document in making pitch decks for seed, then series a and then a due diligence deck.  make sure to search the web for best guidance at each stage and be detailed in all areas. 

[ASSISTANT]
Here is a complete, stage-by-stage guidance document for building investor-grade pitch decks for a mobile game studio. It integrates best practices and fresh external guidance, with load-bearing claims sourced and dated.

# Assumptions
- Audience: venture investors who may not all be games specialists.
- Stages covered: Seed, Series A, and the follow-on Due Diligence deck.
- Format: core deck kept concise, details moved to appendix or data room. YC and DocSend both support short, front-loaded narratives and show investors scan decks quickly. citeturn0search8turn0search0turn0search1

# Brief plan
- Define the narrative spine common to all stages.
- Specify per-stage slide flow, purpose, inputs, and acceptance checks.
- Pin metrics and evidence expectations with current industry references.
- Finish with visual standards, slide count policy, and a due-diligence checklist.

# 1) Narrative spine for all stages
Cover the universal beats that top investors expect: company purpose, problem, solution, market and timing, product, business model, competition, team, financials, and ask. Put “Why now” early. This mirrors Sequoia’s canonical structure and YC’s seed and Series A guides. citeturn0search14turn0search8turn0search0

# 2) Seed deck guide

## Objective
Win a first institutional round by proving: there is a timely opening, the game’s core is compelling, the team can ship, and the plan to first meaningful data is credible.

## Recommended flow and what to prove
1. **Cover**. Studio name, one-line promise, platform focus, founders, contact.
2. **Why now**. Three timing drivers with one chart or timeline. Put dates on each driver. citeturn0search14
3. **Opportunity**. TAM, then a defendable reachable slice for your sub-genre and channels. Keep it conservative.
4. **Player insight**. Two frictions and two desires, shown with short quotes or clips.
5. **The game: 15-second hook**. One-sentence pitch plus continuous gameplay, not a teaser.
6. **Core loop and two deepening systems**. Show how the loop creates retention and spend opportunities.
7. **Distribution advantage**. Channels you can win, creator or community leverage, and early placement access. a16z advises giving non-gaming investors crisp industry context and distribution early. citeturn0search11
8. **Monetisation model**. Pillars, price bands, first-purchase path, ad strategy if used.
9. **Early metrics or a transparent model**. If pre-data, present an LTV model with sensitivity and benchmarks. Label units and time bases. DocSend data shows readers scan fast, so front-load the proof. citeturn0search1
10. **Go-to-market plan**. First 180 days, test geos, creative testing, community beats, stop rules.
11. **Competition**. Three to five nearest comps, and your wedge in fun, distribution, or economics.
12. **Team**. Four to six leaders with two lines tied to shipped titles or scaled live ops.
13. **Roadmap and gates**. First playable, closed alpha, soft launch, with exit criteria and KPI targets.
14. **Financial snapshot**. 18–24 month budget, hiring tranches, UA runway, and target payback logic.
15. **Use of funds**. Pounds by milestone, reconciled to the round size.
16. **Risks and mitigations**. Top five with owners.
17. **Ask**. Round size, instrument, timing, data room link.

## Metrics and evidence that help at seed
- If you have tests: D1, D7, D28 or D30 retention, payer rate, ARPDAU, first purchase timing, by source and platform. GameAnalytics’ 2024–2025 benchmarks are a useful backdrop for context in notes or appendix. Do not overfit to medians since genre and geo matter. citeturn4search12turn4search19
- If pre-data: show a transparent LTV framework and CAC assumptions, with sources for definitions, and sensitivity bands. citeturn4search10
- Keep total core slides inside 10–15 to respect investor scan behaviour. citeturn0search8turn0search1

## Acceptance checks
- One message per slide, one proof per claim.
- Clear timing thesis and distribution path.
- Gates have measurable exit criteria.

# 3) Series A deck guide

## Objective
Show repeatable traction and capital efficiency. Prove that unit economics scale, operations are under control, and the pipeline can sustain growth.

## Recommended flow and what to prove
1. **Cover**.
2. **Why now plus market motion** since seed. Short update on platform or UA changes. YC’s Series A advice stresses tight, data-led narratives. citeturn0search0
3. **Traction summary on one slide**. Installs, DAU/MAU, payers, revenue mix, top geos, and a single chart showing retention or payback progress.
4. **Product deep dive**. Core plus the systems and content cadence that sustain D30 and beyond.
5. **Cohorts and retention curves**. D1, D7, D28 or D30, ideally split by source and platform. Use appendix for full tables. GameAnalytics reports provide contemporary context for the reader. citeturn4search12
6. **Monetisation**. Store architecture, SKU ladder, conversion funnel, ad stack contribution, first-purchase path, and price testing results. AppsFlyer’s 2024 monetisation report is a good reference in notes for ARPU patterns by model. citeturn4search4
7. **Unit economics**. LTV by cohort and channel, CAC by channel, LTV:CAC, and payback windows with a sensitivity band. citeturn4search10
8. **GTM engine**. Creative testing system, channel mix, creator programmes, and scale constraints. AppsFlyer’s creative trends can help frame expectations. citeturn4search24
9. **Technology and operations**. Build pipeline, analytics, experiment framework, live ops tooling, compliance.
10. **Roadmap and scale plan**. New markets, content cadence, feature bets, infra scale.
11. **Team for scale**. Key hires since seed, open roles, and coverage map.
12. **Financials**. Revenue, gross margin drivers, burn, runway, forward plan, and growth scenarios.
13. **Use of funds**. Direct tie to scale plan and milestones.
14. **Risks and mitigations**. Concentration risk, platform policy, UA volatility, content velocity, technical debt.
15. **Ask**.

## Acceptance checks
- Clear path to repeatable payback and LTV:CAC by channel.
- Retention and revenue curves shown as cohorts, not blended.
- Slide count still inside a tight 10–15 for the live narrative, with data depth in appendix. citeturn0search0

# 4) Due Diligence deck guide

## Objective
Enable fast verification. Provide the structured detail investors expect during diligence and eliminate back-and-forth by organising a clean data room.

## Structure and contents
1. **Executive summary**. Three to five bullets with links to sections below.
2. **Company and legal**. Org chart, cap table, equity plans, key contracts, licences. Use a standard VC diligence checklist to ensure coverage. citeturn2search1turn2search5
3. **Product and roadmap**. Design docs, live ops calendars, feature backlogs, pipelines.
4. **Data and analytics**. Event taxonomy, cohort exports, retention and revenue curves by geo, source, platform, and version. Label units, currency, and dates.
5. **Go-to-market**. UA test logs, channel performance, creative taxonomy, creator and community programmes.
6. **Financials**. Historical P&L, cash flow, forecasts, budget by function, vendor schedules, UA spend logs.
7. **Technology**. Architecture, SDK list, CI pipeline, security practices, compliance notes.
8. **People**. Bios, hiring plan, contractors, compensation bands.
9. **Risks and compliance**. Risk register, mitigations, policy alignment, data privacy.
10. **Appendices**. Store pages, reviews, customer support tags, platform communications.

## Data room checklist and hygiene
- Use a clear category tree and version labels. See DocSend and Papermark for current checklists and organisation advice. citeturn2search4turn2search0
- Include early-stage KPI staples: CAC, LTV, payback, burn, churn, and revenue growth where applicable. citeturn2search5
- Provide cohort definitions and methodology for LTV and CAC. Link to a short explainer if needed. citeturn2search13

# 5) Evidence and metrics references for games

Use these as context in notes or appendix. Do not claim them as targets without genre and geo nuance.

- **Retention context**. GameAnalytics benchmark reports publish D1, D7, and D28 medians and breakouts by genre and region. Useful for framing but not as goals. citeturn4search12turn4search19  
- **Monetisation patterns**. AppsFlyer’s monetisation report highlights ARPU differences by model and platform which can inform SKU ladders and projections. citeturn4search4  
- **UA and creative trends**. The share of spend captured by top creatives is high, so show how your testing system searches for winners. citeturn4search24

# 6) Visual, pacing, and file standards

- **Slide count policy**. Live seed and Series A narratives stay within 10–15 slides. Send-ahead PDFs can stretch to 12–18 with an appendix. YC guidance and DocSend scan-time data both support brevity. citeturn0search8turn0search0turn0search1
- **Quality bar**. Use one sans-serif family, titles ≥ 40 pt, body ≥ 20 pt, one message per slide, one primary graphic, and labelled axes with units and dates. Keep gameplay clips to authentic control footage.
- **Accessibility**. Aim for AA contrast and avoid text on busy imagery.
- **Export hygiene**. PDF under roughly 12 MB with embedded fonts. Provide a link to the live deck for meetings.

# 7) Stage-specific acceptance rubrics

## Seed: pass if
- A clear “Why now” with dated drivers. citeturn0search14
- A working representation of the core loop and a credible plan to first external data.
- Team coverage maps to design, engineering, live ops, UA, and data.

## Series A: pass if
- Cohort retention and revenue curves show repeatability, not one-off spikes.
- LTV:CAC and payback windows presented by channel, with sensitivity. citeturn4search10
- Scale plan and use of funds directly tied to content cadence and market expansion.

## Due Diligence: pass if
- Data room follows a recognised checklist structure and contains labelled cohort exports, contracts, financials, and risk logs. citeturn2search4turn2search1

# Self-audit
- Stage guidance aligns with YC’s seed and Series A recommendations and Sequoia’s template beats. citeturn0search8turn0search0turn0search14
- Slide count and pacing align with DocSend’s observed investor scan times. citeturn0search1
- Games-specific evidence references are current and neutral. Benchmarks are framed as context, not hard targets. citeturn4search12turn4search19turn4search4

# 2025 shifts that matter and how to reflect them

1) **Platform fees and distribution in flux, EU-first.**  
   - Apple’s EU terms were overhauled after DMA enforcement, with a new two-tier Store Services fee structure and a Core Technology Commission that replaces the old per-install fee from 1 Jan 2026. If you distribute in the EU via App Store, web, or alternative stores, your economics change. Show the scenario maths and your channel choice. citeturn1news22turn0news61turn0search13  
   - Google and Epic reached a proposed global settlement that lowers standard Play fees and improves access for third-party stores and alternative billing, with remedies proposed to run into the 2030s. Your Android fee stack and go-to-market options expand. Investors will expect you to model both the default Play path and an alt-store path. citeturn1search2turn1search12

   **Deck updates:**  
   - Seed and Series A: in **Monetisation** and **Use of Funds**, add a one-slide **“EU distribution and fees”** box showing iOS tiers vs web vs alt-store, and Android Play vs third-party store. Keep the core simple, put the fee table in the appendix.  
   - DD: include a **Fee & Distribution Assumptions** worksheet and links to the source policies and dates.

2) **Privacy & measurement keep shifting on Android and iOS.**  
   - Android Privacy Sandbox continues to roll out SDK Runtime and related privacy tech. Show how you will attribute performance and run experiments under these APIs. citeturn0search0turn0search12  
   - iOS cloud gaming rules were relaxed in 2024, and Apple’s EU distribution changes complicate attribution and commerce choices. If you touch cloud streaming or companion flows, note compliance and analytics impacts. citeturn0search2turn0search8

   **Deck updates:**

   - DD: provide **Attribution Methodology** docs and experiment logs.

3) **UA channels stabilised but are more automated and creative-led.**

   **Deck updates:**

   **Deck updates:**

5) **UK and EU compliance steps are now table stakes for investor diligence.**

Below is a rebuilt, stage-by-stage guide to making investor-grade pitch decks for a mobile game studio. It is a hands-on tutorial that specifies the narrative, slide structure, visuals, and quality bars for Seed, Series A, and a Due Diligence deck. I have validated the guidance against current 2025 best practice from YC and Sequoia, investor reading-time studies, and games-specific benchmarks and UA trends. Citations are included where they materially shape decisions.

# Assumptions
- Audience includes non-gaming investors. Keep universal investor beats while proving gameplay, economics, and distribution.
- 16:9 slides, British English. Core narrative stays concise, detail moves to appendix and data room. YC recommends keeping core decks tight and Series A narratives within roughly 10–15 slides. citeturn0search0turn0search5
- Investors skim quickly. Plan for about 3 minutes of attention and front-load proof. Recent deck analytics show 3.2 minutes average and strong first-page bias. citeturn0search7

# Brief plan
1) Provide a slide-by-slide tutorial for Seed, Series A, and Due Diligence.  
2) For each slide: Objective, Narrative to drive, What to show, Visual spec, Quality checks, Pitfalls.  
3) Add visual fidelity standards, evidence policy, and acceptance rubrics so a first-time founder can self-grade.

# 0) Universal narrative spine and slide count policy
- Narrative beats investors expect: Purpose, Why now, Market, Product, Business model, Competition, Team, Financials, Ask. This mirrors Sequoia’s canonical pattern and YC’s guidance. citeturn0search1turn0search6turn0search0
- Slide count policy for live talks: 10–15 slides for Seed and Series A. Send-ahead PDFs can stretch to 12–18 plus appendix. YC and multiple datasets support brevity. citeturn0search5turn0search7

# 1) Seed deck tutorial

## Goals at Seed
Prove a timely opening, a compelling core loop, credible distribution, and a plan to first meaningful data. Keep the story simple and visual. YC emphasises one main idea per slide. citeturn0search0

### Slide 1. Cover
- Objective: Establish promise and craft.  
- Narrative: Who you are and the game you are building, in one line.  
- What to show: Studio name, one-line promise, platform focus, founders, contact. One in-engine still or a muted 5–10 second loop.  
- Visual spec: Title ≥ 40 pt; body ≥ 20 pt; single image at 1080p; safe margins 40 px.  
- Quality checks: Real gameplay, not concept art. Optimise media size.  
- Pitfalls: Teaser CG with no input feedback.

### Slide 2. Why now
- Objective: Prove timing advantage.  
- Narrative: Three dated drivers that unlocked this opportunity.  
- What to show: One compact chart or timeline with three drivers tied to outcomes like lower CPI or higher watch-time.  
- Visual spec: One chart, one sentence per driver.  
- Quality checks: All drivers dated and sourced.  
- Pitfalls: Vague macro trends.  
- Sources: YC places strong emphasis on early “why now.” Sequoia template includes “Why Now” explicitly. citeturn0search0turn0search6

### Slide 3. Opportunity
- Objective: Quantify a reachable market slice, not just TAM.  
- Narrative: TAM, then a realistic sub-genre SAM and your initial SOM by channel and geo.  
- What to show: One bar chart and a one-line reachability claim.  
- Visual spec: Bars with labelled units and dates.  
- Quality checks: SOM is bottom-up.  
- Pitfalls: “Percent of TAM” shortcuts.  
- Context: 2025 mobile gaming returned to growth, with regional differences. Use a small 2024 to 2025 motion chart in notes or appendix. citeturn2search1turn2search3

### Slide 4. Player insight
- Objective: Show you understand the player.  
- Narrative: Two frictions and two desires your game addresses.  
- What to show: Two short quotes or clips, one audience definition, one play-occasion.  
- Visual spec: Two tiles with captions ≤ 15 words each.  
- Quality checks: Insights specific to your sub-genre.  
- Pitfalls: Generic personas.

### Slide 5. The game: 15-second hook
- Objective: Convey the core in one breath.  
- Narrative: One sentence that explains genre, camera, primary interaction, average session.  
- What to show: A single 15-second continuous gameplay capture.  
- Visual spec: 1080p MP4, ≤ 15 MB, HUD visible if it clarifies the loop.  
- Quality checks: Real control and feedback.  
- Pitfalls: Jump cuts that hide inputs.

### Slide 6. Core loop and two deepening systems
- Objective: Show how fun becomes retention and spend.  
- Narrative: Three to five verbs in a loop, plus two systems that deepen mastery and create sinks.  
- What to show: Loop diagram, economy sinks and sources list.  
- Visual spec: One loop schematic, two small callouts.  
- Quality checks: Loop matches declared session length.  
- Pitfalls: Too many systems pre-soft launch.

### Slide 7. Distribution advantage
- Objective: Prove access to players beyond raw spend.  
- Narrative: Channel focus, creative angles, community levers, any exclusive placements.  
- What to show: A table with channel, CPI target, scale, creative themes.  
- Visual spec: Clean table ≤ 6 rows.  
- Quality checks: At least one non-paid growth lever and a test plan.  
- Pitfalls: Assuming paid UA will always be available at the modelled CPI.  
- Context: Top creatives still dominate spend; a robust creative testing system is a moat. citeturn0search9turn0search19

### Slide 8. Monetisation model
- Objective: Show value exchange and pricing logic.  
- Narrative: Primary pillars, price bands, the first-purchase path, and ad strategy if used.  
- What to show: Store mockups, SKU ladder, guardrails against pay-to-win.  
- Visual spec: One mockup, one table.  
- Quality checks: First-purchase path is obvious.  
- Pitfalls: Copying a top grossing store without your cadence or audience.

### Slide 9. Early metrics or transparent model
- Objective: Anchor on data, or be explicit with assumptions.  
- Narrative: If tested, show D1, D7, D28 or D30, payer rate, ARPDAU, by source. If pre-data, show an LTV model with sensitivity and benchmarks.  
- What to show: One lead chart, one small cohort table.  
- Visual spec: Lines or cohorts, clearly labelled units and dates.  
- Quality checks: Cohorts not blended, sensitivity bands ±20 percent.  
- Pitfalls: Cherry-picking best cohorts only.  
- Context: Use GameAnalytics benchmarks as context in notes or appendix, not as targets. citeturn0search18

### Slide 10. Go-to-market and measurement plan
- Objective: Show your 180-day learning plan and how you will attribute.  
- Narrative: Test geos, creative testing cadence, community beats, stop rules. Note attribution per channel under SKAN and Android Privacy Sandbox.  
- What to show: Calendar with tests and decision rules.  
- Visual spec: One timetable, one small box for attribution methods.  
- Quality checks: Owner per test; clear stop rules.  
- Pitfalls: Long launch with no gates.  
- References: Android Privacy Sandbox APIs for attribution, and SDK Runtime notes. citeturn1search5turn1search1

### Slide 11. Competition
- Objective: Position against real substitutes.  
- Narrative: Three to five nearest comps and your wedge in fun, distribution, or economics.  
- What to show: One matrix that actually varies on both axes.  
- Visual spec: Simple 2×2, three callouts.  
- Quality checks: Wedge is felt in the loop, not only a brand claim.  
- Pitfalls: Giant grids.

### Slide 12. Team
- Objective: De-risk execution with relevant wins.  
- Narrative: Four to six leaders, each tied to shipped titles or scaled live ops.  
- What to show: Tiles with name, role, two lines of proof.  
- Visual spec: Consistent headshot ratio; tiles aligned to grid.  
- Quality checks: Roles cover design, engineering, live ops, UA, data.  
- Pitfalls: Long bios.

### Slide 13. Roadmap and gates
- Objective: Align capital to outcomes.  
- Narrative: First Playable, Closed Alpha, Soft Launch, each with exit criteria and KPI targets.  
- What to show: Roadmap with four gates and KPIs.  
- Visual spec: Milestone timeline with KPIs beside each gate.  
- Quality checks: Each gate measurable.  
- Pitfalls: Vague words like polish without metrics.

### Slide 14. Financial snapshot
- Objective: Present burn and runway tied to plan.  
- Narrative: 18–24 month budget by function, hiring tranches, UA runway, and payback logic.  
- What to show: Monthly burn line and a small unit economics box.  
- Visual spec: One line chart; one table.  
- Quality checks: Headcount matches milestones.  
- Pitfalls: UA spend before retention gates clear.

### Slide 15. EU distribution and fees summary
- Objective: Reflect 2025 EU fee reality and choices.  
- Narrative: iOS in the EU has new fee structures and a Core Technology Commission coming in 2026; Android settlement proposals may lower fees and expand third-party store options. Show the scenario maths and what you plan to use.  
- What to show: One table comparing iOS EU options and Android default vs alt store.  
- Visual spec: Two columns, clear footnotes to sources and dates.  
- Quality checks: Fees reconcile to the model.  
- Pitfalls: Ignoring EU distribution choices.  
- References: Apple EU support pages on CTF and future CTC, plus EU summary; Google–Epic proposed settlement coverage. citeturn1search0turn1search4turn1search12turn1search6turn1search10

### Slide 16. Risks and mitigations
- Objective: Build trust by naming the dragons.  
- Narrative: Top five risks with owners and specific mitigations.  
- What to show: Five rows, owners, next check.  
- Visual spec: Simple table.  
- Quality checks: Specific, not clichés.

### Slide 17. The ask
- Objective: Make it easy to progress.  
- Narrative: Round size, instrument, timing, data room link.  
- What to show: One box with terms and contact.  
- Visual spec: Large, clear contact line.

## Seed acceptance rubric
- Pass if Why now is dated and sourced, distribution plan includes creative testing, and EU fee choices are reflected in economics. YC slide count guidance met. citeturn0search0

# 2) Series A deck tutorial

## Goals at Series A
Prove repeatable traction, capital efficiency, and scale readiness. YC’s Series A guidance stresses tight data-led narratives within 10–15 slides. citeturn0search5

### Slide 1. Cover
As Seed. Consistent look.

- Objective: Show platform and market motion since Seed.

# 3) Due Diligence deck tutorial

## Goals in diligence
Enable fast verification. Organise detail in a clean data room with labelled cohorts, contracts, risks, and compliance. DocSend-style review flows benefit from clear file structure and analytics. citeturn0search17

- Objective: Three to five bullets with links to sections.  
- Visual: One page of links.

### Section B. Company and legal
- Objective: Reduce legal uncertainty quickly.

- Visual: Tabular glossary and link list.

### Section C. Product and roadmap
- Contents: Design docs, live-ops calendars, backlog exports, build pipeline screenshot.

### Section D. Data and analytics
- Contents: Cohort exports by geo, source, platform, version with attribution method noted. Retention and revenue curves with labelled units and dates.  
- Visual: Annotated charts.  
- References: Privacy Sandbox attribution docs in notes if relevant. citeturn1search5

- Contents: UA test logs, channel performance, creative taxonomy and results, creator programmes.  
- References: Creative concentration data in notes. citeturn0search9

- Contents: Historical P&L, cash flow, forecast, budget by function, vendor schedules, UA spend logs.

### Section G. Technology
- Contents: Architecture, SDK list, CI pipeline, security and data privacy notes.

### Section H. People
- Contents: Bios, hiring plan, contractors, compensation bands.

### Section I. Compliance and safety
- Contents: Online Safety Act posture for UGC or chat, age-assurance plan and vendor, moderation workflows, EU DMA choices and fee tier rationale for iOS, Android settlement implications.

- Store pages, reviews, CS tags, platform communications.

## Diligence acceptance rubric
- Pass if fee and distribution workbook is present with sources and dates, cohorts are labelled with methods, and safety documents match Ofcom guidance. citeturn1search0turn1search4turn1search3

# 4) Visual fidelity, pacing, and file hygiene standards
- Canvas: 16:9 at 1920×1080. Safe margins 40 px. Use a 12-column grid or a simple 4-column grid.  
- Palette: One primary, one secondary, one highlight, two neutrals. Keep charts to six categories.  
- Type: One sans-serif family. Title ≥ 40 pt, headings ≥ 28 pt, body ≥ 20 pt, labels ≥ 16 pt.  
- Contrast: Aim for WCAG AA.  
- Density: One message per slide, three to five short lines, one primary graphic. YC warns against multiple ideas per slide. citeturn0search5
- Gameplay media: Authentic input and feedback, 1080p, ≤ 15 MB per clip for send-ahead.  
- Charts: Axes start at zero unless annotated, units and dates required, no 3D, annotate one key insight on the chart.  
- Animation: Keep to appear or fade, 200–300 ms, export final states to PDF.  
- Export hygiene: PDF ≤ roughly 12 MB with embedded fonts. Live deck retained for meetings.

# 5) Evidence and metrics references you can cite in notes or appendix
- Investor-grade slide structure: YC Seed and YC Series A. citeturn0search0turn0search5  
- Sequoia template beats and Why Now. citeturn0search1turn0search6  
- Investor scan behaviour and optimal ranges. citeturn0search7  
- Games benchmarks context: GameAnalytics 2024. citeturn0search18  
- UA and creative concentration: AppsFlyer 2025. citeturn0search9

- Platform fees and distribution choices: Apple EU support pages and EU summary; Google–Epic proposed settlement. citeturn1search0turn1search4turn1search12turn1search18  
- UK Online Safety Act guidance for games and age checks. citeturn1search3turn1search11

# 6) Tutorial add-ons for first-time founders

## A. Slide drafting workflow
1) Write the title as the takeaway sentence. If you remove the body, the titles alone should tell the full story end-to-end. Many investors recommend this headline-as-story approach. citeturn0search11  
2) For each slide, list one claim and one proof.  
3) Replace filler with evidence. If you have no evidence, move the claim to the roadmap with a test plan.

## B. Asset checklist per slide
- Cover: logo, working build capture, founder names, contact.  
- Game hook: 15 seconds of real gameplay, not a trailer.  
- Core loop: diagram plus two UI screenshots that show the verbs.  
- Metrics: cohort CSV extract, labelled and dated.  
- GTM: creative taxonomy page and next ten tests.  
- EU fees: one worksheet with fee options and assumptions, dated sources.

## C. Quality control loop
- Run a slide-by-slide QA: message clarity, evidence, readability, data labelling, template alignment.  
- Use an acceptance rubric per stage and record a simple pass, minor fixes, or fail per slide.  
- Track changes. Version the deck and add a one-line change log.

# 7) Self-critique of this guide
Readability: Each slide has a single objective, a narrative to drive, and concrete “what to show.” Visual specs and file hygiene are explicit. The structure mirrors investor expectations and YC’s emphasis on simplicity and tight slide counts. citeturn0search5  
Completeness: The guide covers Seed, Series A, and Due Diligence with acceptance rubrics, asset checklists, and measurement plans under iOS and Android Privacy Sandbox. citeturn1search5  
2025 alignment: EU App Store fee options and prospective Android fee reforms are included with sources, and UK Online Safety Act expectations for UGC or chat are covered for diligence. citeturn1search0turn1search4turn1search12turn1search18turn1search3  
Potential gaps: Market figures are referenced to current reports rather than reproduced. For a working deck, add your own SOM bottom-up model and a fee scenario worksheet tailored to your channels.  
Quality risks: First-time teams often over-explain on slides and bury the hook. Use headline-as-story and keep to one idea per slide to stay within the scan window. citeturn0search5turn0search7

[ASSISTANT]
Here is a clean glossary you can append to the deck-building guide. It defines each acronym, gives the exact meaning in a games pitch context, and, where useful, includes a formula, a usage note for slides, and a common pitfall to avoid.

# Acronyms and definitions for a mobile games pitch (2025)

## A. Market, finance, and investor metrics
**TAM**  
Meaning: Total Addressable Market. The full revenue opportunity for your broad category.  
Slide use: Keep TAM on one chart, but drive to SAM and SOM.  
Pitfall: Claiming a flat percentage of TAM as your plan.

**SAM**  
Meaning: Serviceable Available Market. The slice of TAM your sub-genre and platforms can realistically serve.  
Slide use: Define by genre, platform, regions.  
Pitfall: Copying a competitor’s SAM without matching channels or regions.

**SOM**  
Meaning: Serviceable Obtainable Market. The part of SAM you can win in the next 24 to 36 months given your channels and budget.  
Slide use: Bottom-up only.  
Pitfall: Treating SOM as a fixed percent of SAM.

**LTV**  
Meaning: Lifetime Value per user. Expected net revenue from acquisition to churn.  
Formula: Sum of net revenue per period, discounted if you model monthly cohorts.  
Slide use: Show LTV by channel and cohort with sensitivity bands.  
Pitfall: Blended LTV across channels or platforms.

**CAC**  
Meaning: Cost to Acquire a paying or all user, depending on your definition.  
Formula: Total acquisition spend ÷ users acquired.  
Slide use: Always label if CAC is per payer or per install.  
Pitfall: Mixing definitions between slides.

**LTV:CAC**  
Meaning: Ratio of value created to cost of acquisition.  
Rule of thumb: ≥ 3 can be healthy, but context matters by genre and geo.  
Pitfall: Presenting a single blended ratio.

**Payback period**  
Meaning: Time to recover CAC from net receipts.  
Formula: Earliest day or month when cumulative net revenue per user ≥ CAC.  
Slide use: Show by channel with a window, not a single point.  
Pitfall: Excluding platform fees or ad-net fees.

**ARPU**  
Meaning: Average Revenue Per User over a period.  
Formula: Period revenue ÷ active users in that period.  
Pitfall: Mixing daily ARPU with monthly ARPU without labels.

**ARPPU**  
Meaning: Average Revenue Per Paying User.  
Formula: Period revenue from payers ÷ number of payers.  
Pitfall: Using ARPPU to imply overall monetisation strength when payer rate is low.

**ARPDAU**  
Meaning: Average Revenue Per Daily Active User.  
Formula: Day revenue ÷ DAU.  
Slide use: Good for day-level comparisons in soft launch.  
Pitfall: Comparing ARPDAU across very different geos or ad densities.

**Gross vs net**  
Gross: Store-side receipts before platform fees and taxes.  
Net: After platform fees, refunds, ad-net fees.  
Slide use: Label which you are using.  
Pitfall: Calling gross “revenue” in a P&L slide.

**COGS**  
Meaning: Cost of Goods Sold. For F2P games, includes hosting, CDN, payment processing, fraud, support vendors.  
Pitfall: Treating UA as COGS. Keep it in Operating Expenses unless you follow a specific policy and label it.

**GM**  
Meaning: Gross Margin.  
Formula: (Revenue − COGS) ÷ Revenue.  
Pitfall: Leaving platform fees out of COGS when you present margins.

## B. Audience, behaviour, and retention
**DAU / WAU / MAU**  
Meaning: Daily, Weekly, Monthly Active Users.  
Pitfall: Counting anyone who opened the app vs users with valid sessions. State your rule.

**D0, D1, D7, D28 or D30**  
Meaning: Retention checkpoints measured from first day of install.  
Slide use: Show cohort curves, not just single percentages.  
Pitfall: Presenting blended retention across channels and platforms.

**Churn**  
Meaning: Users who stop being active within a period.  
Formula example (monthly): 1 − retention rate for that cohort in that month.  
Pitfall: Using churn without a clear time base.

**Cohort**  
Meaning: A group of users sharing a start attribute, such as install week, channel, or geo.  
Slide use: Label cohorts by start date, channel, platform, and app version.

**FTUE**  
Meaning: First-Time User Experience. The onboarding and tutorial sequence.  
Slide use: Show completion rate and time to first fun or first purchase.  
Pitfall: Measuring only tutorial completion without linking to D1 retention.

**Payer rate**  
Meaning: Percent of active users who made at least one purchase in the period or since install.  
Label clearly: period payer rate vs cumulative payer rate.

## C. UA, advertising, and creative performance
**UA**  
Meaning: User Acquisition. Paid and non-paid activities to grow installs or registrations.

**CPI / CPA**  
CPI: Cost Per Install. CPA: Cost Per Action (define the action).  
Pitfall: Calling a CPA a CPI or vice versa.

**ROAS**  
Meaning: Return On Ad Spend.  
Formula: Attributed revenue ÷ ad spend over a window (eg D7 ROAS, D30 ROAS).  
Pitfall: Comparing ROAS windows that differ by channel.

**CTR / CVR**  
CTR: Click-Through Rate. CVR: Conversion Rate to install or purchase.  
Pitfall: Not stating which conversion you mean.

**eCPM**  
Meaning: Effective Cost Per Mille (thousand) impressions on ads.  
Pitfall: Comparing eCPMs across very different geos without labels.

**Ad LTV**  
Meaning: Lifetime ad revenue per user.  
Slide use: Separate from IAP LTV in decks.

**Mediation, waterfall, in-app bidding**  
Mediation: Layer that routes ad requests across networks.  
Waterfall: Fixed priority order.  
In-app bidding: Real-time auction.  
Slide use: State which you use and why.

**K-factor**  
Meaning: Viral coefficient. Average number of additional users each user brings.  
Formula: Invites per user × conversion rate to install.  
Pitfall: Assuming K > 1 without evidence.

## D. Product, monetisation, and systems
**IAP / IAA**  
IAP: In-App Purchases. IAA: In-App Advertising.  
Slide use: Show the split.

**BP**  
Meaning: Battle Pass. Time-bound progression track with free and premium tiers.  
Pitfall: No clear first-purchase path that leads into BP.

**SKU**  
Meaning: Stock Keeping Unit. Distinct purchasable item or bundle.  
Slide use: Present a ladder from entry price to whale products.

**Gacha**  
Meaning: Randomised item draw mechanic with published odds where required.  
Compliance: Show odds where needed by store or region.

**Economy sinks and sources**  
Sources: Ways to earn currency.  
Sinks: Ways currency leaves the economy.  
Slide use: Present both on the core loop slide.

**Session length and cadence**  
Meaning: Typical time per session and sessions per day.  
Slide use: Align loop complexity to declared cadence.

## E. Attribution, privacy, and platform policy
**SKAN**  
Meaning: Apple’s SKAdNetwork for iOS attribution without IDFA.  
Slide use: Note which version you target and what windows you report.  
Pitfall: Reporting channel LTV without stating the attribution method.

**IDFA / ATT**  
IDFA: Identifier for Advertisers.  
ATT: App Tracking Transparency.  
Slide use: If relevant, show opt-in rates.

**Android Privacy Sandbox**  
Key parts: Attribution Reporting API, Topics API, SDK Runtime.  
Slide use: State what you test and how you judge success under these constraints.

**DMA / DSA**  
DMA: EU Digital Markets Act.  
DSA: EU Digital Services Act.  
Slide use: Mention if your distribution or moderation choices are shaped by these.

**OSA**  
Meaning: UK Online Safety Act.  
Slide use: If you have UGC or chat, include a safety posture and age-assurance note in diligence.

**CTF / CTC**  
CTF: Apple’s Core Technology Fee for certain EU distribution choices.  
CTC: Apple’s Core Technology Commission planned to replace per-install fees on a future date per Apple’s EU framework.  
Slide use: Include a fee scenario table and margin bridge for the EU.

## F. Technology and operations
**SDK**  
Meaning: Software Development Kit.  
Slide use: List critical SDKs in the tech slide or appendix.

**API**  
Meaning: Application Programming Interface.  
Slide use: Only list when it shows an integration that matters for scale or compliance.

**CI / CD**  
CI: Continuous Integration.  
CD: Continuous Delivery or Deployment.  
Slide use: One diagram showing build pipeline and release cadence.

**SLA**  
Meaning: Service Level Agreement.  
Slide use: If uptime or moderation vendor performance is material, include the SLA target in diligence.

**KPI**  
Meaning: Key Performance Indicator.  
Slide use: Tie each roadmap gate to one KPI with a target.

## G. Store and billing
**1P / 3P billing**  
1P: First-party store billing.  
3P: Third-party or alternative billing where allowed.  
Slide use: Note if and where you plan to use it and show fee impact.

**Alt store**  
Meaning: Alternative app store distribution.  
Slide use: Present as an option with clear pros, cons, and fee impact.

## H. Common chart and table labels for your slides
- Units: always state currency, date range, and whether amounts are gross or net.  
- Time bases: day, week, or month, and whether cohorts align to install date or calendar date.  
- Methods: state attribution method per channel on metrics slides.

# Quick reference: formulas to keep consistent
- ARPU = revenue in period ÷ active users in period  
- ARPDAU = revenue on day ÷ DAU on day  
- ARPPU = revenue from payers in period ÷ number of payers in period  
- CAC = acquisition spend ÷ users acquired (define user type)  
- Payback window = earliest day/month when cumulative net revenue per user ≥ CAC  
- LTV (simplified) = sum of expected net revenue per user over time, discounted if monthly  
- Payer rate (period) = payers in period ÷ active users in period  
- Retention Dn = percent of a cohort active on day n after install

# Usage guidance inside the deck
- On first appearance of any acronym in the core deck, write it out once in full in the subtitle or a footnote, then use the acronym thereafter.  
- In charts, keep the long form in the axis title and the acronym in the legend, for example: “Average Revenue Per Daily Active User (ARPDAU), USD.”

# Quality guardrails for acronym use
- One definition per acronym across the whole deck and appendix.  
- Keep a one-page “Acronym key” in the appendix, and link it in the table of contents.  
- If you present LTV, CAC, or payback, include a caption with your method choices: cohort alignment, attribution method, net or gross, and fee assumptions.

Glen, here’s a clean, stand-alone appendix you can bolt onto the guide: platform-specific guidance and KPI gates. It treats each platform as a distribution channel you can test, not a foregone bet.

# Platform Annex: Channel-specific guidance and KPI gates

## How to use this annex
- Pick the platform you will test next.  
- Copy the “Experiment design” block, fill it, and run a 2 to 4 week test.  
- Judge with the pass/fail gates provided. If you pass, scale. If you fail, iterate or stop.

### Experiment design template
- Objective: what decision this test must unlock.  
- Audience and geo: target cohort, language, store region.  
- Entry points: where traffic starts on the platform.  
- Tracking: the legal and technical measurement stack.  
- Creative set: number of variants and hooks.  
- Budget and caps: ceiling per day and per variant.  
- Success gates: the exact thresholds below.  
- Next action: scale plan if gates are met.

---

## Telegram Mini Apps
**What matters**  
- Reach and growth live inside Telegram’s chat graph. Distribution comes from channels, groups, bot discovery, deep links, and Telegram Ads. Mini Apps are Web Apps launched inside Telegram with JS APIs for auth, payments, fullscreen, theming, and device capabilities. Stars enable in-Telegram purchases for digital goods and subscriptions. citeturn1search0turn1news21turn1search8

**Monetisation options**  
- Telegram Stars for digital goods and subs; third-party payment providers also supported. Plan refund flows and dispute handling; Telegram may claw back Stars on upheld disputes. citeturn1search8turn1search0turn1search2

**Attribution and analytics**  
- No IDFA. Treat as owned-and-operated web with UTMs, per-message deep links, and server-side events. Pair with Telegram Ads reporting and your backend events.

**Common failure modes**  
- Treating Telegram like an app store. It is more like a social distribution surface with fast FTUE and viral loops.

**KPI gates for a 2-week Alpha**  
- Landing to start: ≥ 60 percent press Start in the Mini App.  
- FTUE completion: ≥ 70 percent finish the first session tutorial.  
- D1 retention: ≥ 30 percent.  
- First purchase rate: ≥ 1.0 percent or Star subscription attach ≥ 0.5 percent in pay-eligible cohorts.  
- Viral coefficient K: ≥ 0.15 in channels with invite mechanics.  
- Ad efficiency: Telegram Ads CPM to cost-per-start sustainable at your unit economics; hold payback ≤ 120 days for early scale.

**Scale gates (4 to 6 weeks)**  
- D7 ≥ 12 percent, payer ARPPU trend stable week-over-week, refund rate on Stars < 2 percent.

---

## Poki (web)
**What matters**  
- Poki runs a staged test pipeline. The Web Fit Test pushes ~10,000 players to estimate audience fit, measuring CTR on category pages, conversion to first gameplayStart, and early engagement. Monetisation is ad-driven through the Poki SDK with rewarded and commercial breaks after testing. Final review takes around 1–2 weeks. citeturn2search2turn2search11turn2search18turn2search4

**Attribution and analytics**  
- Use Poki SDK events for CTR, conversion to play, first session duration, and ad break readiness. Treat as pure web funnel.

**Common failure modes**  
- Slow load and heavy bundles, weak thumbnails, long time-to-first-action.

**KPI gates for Web Fit Test**  
- Category CTR: aim for top quartile in your genre on Poki.  
- Conversion to play: ≥ 70 percent after first gameplayStart event.  
- First session median: ≥ 6 minutes in casual, ≥ 10 minutes in arcade/strategy.  
- Build health: time-to-first-interaction ≤ 3 seconds on mid-range laptops.

**Scale gates**  
- Featured exposure trial shows stable engagement uplift and rewardedBreak acceptance rate ≥ 35 percent without core loop drop-off.

Note: Poki does not publish a public, fixed revenue share; it is negotiated and may vary with exclusivity and performance. Plan with ranges and confirm in contract. citeturn2search1

---

## iOS (App Store)
**What matters**  
- Measurement relies on SKAdNetwork and AdAttributionKit, not device-level IDs. Expect up to three postbacks across conversion windows in SKAN 4+, with hierarchical conversion values. Loot box odds disclosure is required in App Review Guidelines 3.1.1. citeturn3search0turn3search6turn3search2turn3search1  
- In the EU, alternative distribution and payment options exist under DMA-driven “alternative business terms,” including a Core Technology Fee for very high scale and tiered fees; check current Apple pages before modelling. citeturn0search11turn0search3

**Attribution and analytics**  
- SKAN 4 mapping, coarse conversion strategies by window, ASA for branded search, in-app analytics for funnel.

**Common failure modes**  
- Mis-configured SKAN schemas, missing odds disclosures, overstuffed first session.

**KPI gates for soft-launch**  
- D1 ≥ 35 percent, D7 ≥ 12 percent for mid-core; tutorial completion ≥ 80 percent.  
- Pay conversion ≥ 1.5 percent in the first paid cohort, or sub trial start ≥ 2.5 percent if using subs.  
- ASA CPT to blended Day-30 ROAS ≥ 70 percent of target with SKAN validated.  
- Compliance: loot box odds disclosed, age ratings correct, ATT prompts only if needed.

**Scale gates**  
- Payback to ≤ 180 days on blended channels at target margin after App Store fees and, where applicable, EU CTF modelling.

---

## Android (Google Play and beyond)
**What matters**  
- Privacy Sandbox on Android changes attribution to on-device APIs, phasing out cross-party identifiers. Plan for Aggregatable Reports and Attribution Reporting. citeturn0search4turn0search12  
- As of Nov 2025, a proposed Epic-Google settlement introduces a global “Registered App Stores” program and lower Play service fee caps of 20 percent or 9 percent depending on transaction type and install context, subject to court approval and rollout rules. Build your cost model from the published ranges until finalised. citeturn5view1

**Attribution and analytics**  
- Privacy Sandbox for ads, Google Analytics for Firebase for product analytics, plus server-side revenue events.

**Common failure modes**  
- Assuming GAID-based user-level attribution, ignoring sandbox reporting lags.

**KPI gates for soft-launch**  
- D1 ≥ 35 percent, D7 ≥ 12 percent mid-core.  
- First purchase ≥ 1.2 percent or sub trial ≥ 2.0 percent.  
- Sandbox-based Day-7 ROAS proxy ≥ 40 percent of target with confidence bands.  
- Store listing experiments yield +10 percent conversion uplift before scale.

**Scale gates**  
- Payback ≤ 180 days on blended Android channels, fees modelled per current Play terms and any alternative billing deltas.

---

## Steam (PC)
**What matters**  
- Revenue share tiers step down beyond 10M and 50M lifetime gross on Steam. Wishlists, followers, demo performance, and participation in Next Fest heavily influence launch momentum. citeturn4search6turn4search12turn4search1

**Attribution and analytics**  
- Steamworks reports, page traffic, demo-to-wishlist conversion, festival uplift. Benchmarks suggest large variance in wishlist-to-week-one sales; treat ratios as directional, not promises. citeturn4search5turn4search20

**Common failure modes**  
- Late demo, weak capsule art, no festival plan, ignoring page A/B.

**KPI gates**  
- Pre-launch: ≥ 20,000 wishlists for premium indie, or ≥ 50,000 for ambitious mid-core.  
- Next Fest: demo-to-wishlist conversion ≥ 25 percent, stream peak concurrence aligned to top 25 percent of your sub-genre.  
- Launch week: wishlist-to-sale ratio 0.15x to 0.30x as a sanity range, adjust for price and EA. citeturn4search5

**Scale gates**  
- Post-launch review score ≥ 85 percent positive with ≥ 500 reviews by Day 14, refund rate under 8 percent.

---

## Cross-platform checklists

### Visual and fidelity requirements
- Trailer per platform: aspect ratio, subtitles on, platform logo slates, 6 to 12 second hook.  
- Capsule art and thumbnails: A/B at least 4 variants where the platform supports it.  
- Performance: time-to-first-interaction budget defined per device class.  
- Accessibility: readable font sizes and contrast; captioned trailers.

### Compliance flashpoints
- Loot box probability disclosures and pricing clarity on iOS. citeturn3search1  
- Payment and refund terms for Telegram Stars visible and handled in your T&Cs. citeturn1search2  
- Privacy Sandbox measuring windows and reporting delays on Android. citeturn0search4  
- Steam tax and reporting differences vs ad-hoc dashboard numbers. citeturn4search15

---

## Quick reference: experiment gates by platform
| Platform | Pass gates to continue |
|---|---|
| Telegram Mini App | Start rate ≥ 60 percent, FTUE ≥ 70 percent, D1 ≥ 30 percent, First purchase ≥ 1.0 percent, K ≥ 0.15, Stars refunds < 2 percent |
| Poki | Category CTR top quartile, Conversion to play ≥ 70 percent, First session median ≥ 6–10 minutes, TTFI ≤ 3 s |
| iOS | D1 ≥ 35 percent, D7 ≥ 12 percent, pay conversion ≥ 1.5 percent or sub trials ≥ 2.5 percent, ASA Day-30 ROAS ≥ 70 percent of target, compliance clean |
| Android | D1 ≥ 35 percent, D7 ≥ 12 percent, first purchase ≥ 1.2 percent, Sandbox Day-7 ROAS proxy ≥ 40 percent of target, listing A/B +10 percent |
| Steam | Pre-launch wishlists ≥ 20k to 50k by scope, Next Fest demo→wishlist ≥ 25 percent, launch wishlist→sale ≈ 0.15x–0.30x, ≥ 85 percent positive reviews |

Numbers are practical bands to decide go or no-go. Calibrate by genre and price point.
