# Novice Edition: Mobile Game Pitch Deck Guide 2025  
Seed, Series A, and Due Diligence

## How to use this guide
- Each subsection equals one slide in Gamma.  
- For every slide you get: Purpose, What investors look for, Gather this first, Build steps, Worked example, Visual spec, Checks, Mistakes to avoid.  
- Define acronyms the first time you use them. A full glossary sits at the end.

---

# Seed Deck Tutorial (15 slides)

## 1. Cover: Title and promise
**Purpose**  
Tell who you are, what the game is, and the one thing players will get.

**What investors look for**  
Real in-engine quality, a clear promise, and quick context.

**Gather this first**  
Logo, one in-engine screenshot or 5 to 10 second gameplay loop, studio and founder names, contact email.

**Build steps**  
1) Write a one-line promise. Example frame: “Build and raid in ten minute sessions on mobile.”  
2) List platforms in small text.  
3) Place your best in-engine visual.  
4) Add founder names and contact.

**Worked example**  
Title: “Iron Harbor Games”  
Subtitle: “Short session strategy on iOS and Android”  
Promise line: “Build, raid, and outsmart rivals in ten minute sessions.”

**Visual spec**  
16:9, 1920x1080, safe margins 40 px, Title 40 pt or larger, Body 24 pt or larger.

**Checks**  
Image is from a working build. Text is readable at 100 percent zoom.

**Mistakes to avoid**  
Do not use cinematic teaser art. Do not crowd the slide.

---

## 2. Why now
**Purpose**  
Prove the timing advantage.

**What investors look for**  
Three dated facts that change reach, cost, or demand.

**Gather this first**  
Recent market datapoints with dates, and one sentence on what each unlocks.

**Build steps**  
1) Pick three facts with dates.  
2) For each, add one outcome line that ties to your game.  
3) Use a simple mini timeline or three small bars.

**Worked example**  
- “Short strategy watch time up 19 percent since 2024. Supports ten minute sessions.”  
- “EU iOS fee options live. Margin improves with alternative terms.”  
- “Android Privacy Sandbox stable. Reporting windows predictable for tests.”

**Visual spec**  
One timeline with three labels. Font 20 to 24 pt.

**Checks**  
Each fact has a date and a direct outcome.

**Mistakes to avoid**  
Vague trends, no dates.

---

## 3. Market size with a reachable slice
**Purpose**  
Show ambition and a realistic first target.

**What investors look for**  
TAM, SAM, SOM with a bottom-up SOM.

**Gather this first**  
Market numbers with dates, your channels, starting prices or expected ARPDAU.

**Build steps**  
1) Bars for TAM, SAM, SOM with units and dates.  
2) One sentence under the chart on how you will reach SOM.  
3) Add the simple math in a caption.

**Worked example**  
“TAM £3.5B mobile strategy worldwide, 2024. SAM £900M English and Western Europe. SOM £7.2M year one from 600k reachable users at £12 LTV.”

**Visual spec**  
Bars with labels. Source line bottom right.

**Checks**  
Units and dates shown. SOM is not a flat percent of TAM.

**Mistakes to avoid**  
Double counting platforms, no source note.

---

## 4. Player problem and desire
**Purpose**  
Prove you understand the player’s pains and hopes.

**What investors look for**  
Specific frictions and desires tied to your loop.

**Gather this first**  
Two quotes or clips from playtests, a short audience note.

**Build steps**  
1) Write two pains, two desires, one sentence each.  
2) Add a player quote or a 3 second clip.  
3) State when and where they play.

**Worked example**  
Pain: “Mobile strategy feels grindy after day 3.”  
Desire: “Smart raids with quick wins during commutes.”

**Visual spec**  
Two tiles with captions under 15 words.

**Checks**  
Subgenre is named. The desire links to your loop.

**Mistakes to avoid**  
Generic personas.

---

## 5. The 15 second hook
**Purpose**  
Make the core interaction obvious.

**What investors look for**  
Continuous gameplay that shows input and response.

**Gather this first**  
One 15 second capture from a working build.

**Build steps**  
1) Record one loop.  
2) Add a caption with genre, camera, session length, capture date.  
3) Do not cut away.

**Worked example**  
Caption: “Base build, scout, raid. Top-down. Two taps to deploy. Average session 10 minutes.”

**Visual spec**  
1080p MP4, 15 MB or less. HUD visible if it clarifies.

**Checks**  
Input and feedback are visible.

**Mistakes to avoid**  
CG footage, montage cuts.

---

## 6. Core loop and two systems
**Purpose**  
Show how fun deepens into retention and spend.

**What investors look for**  
A compact loop and two systems that add mastery.

**Gather this first**  
Loop diagram, names of one source and one sink in the economy, two UI screenshots.

**Build steps**  
1) Draw a 3 to 5 verb loop.  
2) Add two systems that lengthen engagement.  
3) Label one source and one sink.

**Worked example**  
Loop: “Build → Scout → Raid → Upgrade.”  
Systems: “Clan wars” and “Seasonal tech tree.”  
Source and sink: “Shards in raids” and “Shard sink in upgrades.”

**Visual spec**  
One loop diagram, two small callouts.

**Checks**  
Loop fits a ten minute session. Systems are additive.

**Mistakes to avoid**  
Feature bloat before soft launch.

---

## 7. Distribution advantage
**Purpose**  
Show how you reach players beyond raw ad spend.

**What investors look for**  
Channels, creative angles, creator plan, realistic CPI targets.

**Gather this first**  
Channel list, creator prospects, community plan.

**Build steps**  
1) Table with channel, CPI target, expected scale, next test.  
2) One line on a creator or community lever.

**Worked example**  
“TikTok strategy creators with base design challenges. Target CPI £0.45. Scale 50k installs in test geo.”

**Visual spec**  
Table under six rows.

**Checks**  
At least one non-paid lever. A test owner is named.

**Mistakes to avoid**  
Assuming any CPI without a plan to test it.

---

## 8. Monetisation model
**Purpose**  
Show a fair value exchange and a clear first purchase path.

**What investors look for**  
SKU ladder, price bands, sinks and sources, ad placement if used.

**Gather this first**  
Store mockup, price points, bundle ideas, first purchase timing.

**Build steps**  
1) Draw the SKU ladder.  
2) Mark the first purchase path and timing.  
3) Place ad positions if applicable and justify them.  
4) State guardrails against pay to win.

**Worked example**  
“First purchase at 30 minutes for £2.99 unlocks Season Path tier one. Early sink is Tier 2 upgrades.”

**Visual spec**  
One store mockup and one simple table.

**Checks**  
Pricing matches session length and sinks.

**Mistakes to avoid**  
Copying a top-grossing store without fit.

---

## 9. Early metrics or a transparent model
**Purpose**  
Anchor on facts or state assumptions clearly.

**What investors look for**  
D1, D7, D30, payer rate, ARPDAU, or a clear LTV model with sensitivity.

**Gather this first**  
Cohort table exports, or model inputs with ranges.

**Build steps**  
1) Lead chart for retention or LTV.  
2) Tiny cohort table with install week, sizes, and method note.  
3) Sensitivity band ± 20 percent on key drivers.

**Worked example**  
“Week 32 iOS cohort D1 36 percent, D7 13 percent. LTV day 90 model £1.60 with £1.28 to £1.92 band.”

**Visual spec**  
Axes labelled with units and dates.

**Checks**  
No blended cohorts. Attribution method stated.

**Mistakes to avoid**  
Cherry picking a best week without context.

---

## 10. Go to market for the next 180 days
**Purpose**  
Show how you will learn fast and spend wisely.

**What investors look for**  
Test geos, creative cadence, decision rules, owners.

**Gather this first**  
Twelve week test calendar, creative backlog, community beats.

**Build steps**  
1) Timeline of experiments with owners.  
2) For each experiment, define stop or scale rule.  
3) Add a short Discord or creator beat plan.

**Worked example**  
“Week 4 exit rule: D7 12 percent or higher unlocks price test A on iOS.”

**Visual spec**  
One timetable and a small box for attribution method.

**Checks**  
Decision rules written as numbers. Owners named.

**Mistakes to avoid**  
Long launch with no gates.

---

## 11. Competitive landscape and your wedge
**Purpose**  
Place yourself among real substitutes.

**What investors look for**  
Three to five comps and a practical wedge.

**Gather this first**  
Three comps, one wedge in loop, distribution, or economics.

**Build steps**  
1) Simple 2 by 2 or a small comparison table.  
2) One callout on the wedge.

**Worked example**  
“Wedge: short session raids with creator-designed challenges that surface daily.”

**Visual spec**  
Keep it readable at 24 pt.

**Checks**  
Wedge is visible in the loop or plan, not only in brand.

**Mistakes to avoid**  
Giant feature grids.

---

## 12. Team fit
**Purpose**  
De-risk execution.

**What investors look for**  
Four to six leaders with shipped outcomes.

**Gather this first**  
Two line bios tied to titles shipped or scaled live ops.

**Build steps**  
1) Place headshots with two lines each.  
2) Cover loop, economy, live ops, data, UA.

**Worked example**  
“Lead Economy Designer: scaled ARPPU 22 percent in 9 months on Title X.”

**Visual spec**  
Consistent tiles and alignment.

**Checks**  
Coverage gaps are visible and honest.

**Mistakes to avoid**  
Celebrity advisors with no time.

---

## 13. Milestones and gates
**Purpose**  
Tie capital to outcomes.

**What investors look for**  
Past proof and next four gates with KPI exits.

**Gather this first**  
Milestone plan with KPI targets and owners.

**Build steps**  
1) Timeline with four gates.  
2) KPI exit criteria next to each gate.  
3) Owner per gate.

**Worked example**  
“Soft launch 1 exits when D1 35 percent, tutorial 80 percent, crash rate under 1 percent.”

**Checks**  
KPIs are specific and measurable.

**Mistakes to avoid**  
Words like polish without numbers.

---

## 14. Financial snapshot
**Purpose**  
Validate the ask and survivability.

**What investors look for**  
18 to 24 month budget by function, hiring tranches, UA runway, months of cash, unit economics summary.

**Gather this first**  
Headcount plan, vendor quotes, UA assumptions.

**Build steps**  
1) Burn line by month.  
2) Small table for unit economics targets and payback.  
3) Note that UA follows retention gates.

**Worked example**  
“Runway 20 months at planned burn. Unit target: LTV £1.80 by day 180, payback 150 days on blended channels.”

**Checks**  
Reconciles month by month. Hiring matches milestones.

**Mistakes to avoid**  
UA spend before retention is stable.

---

## 15. The ask
**Purpose**  
Make next steps easy.

**What investors look for**  
Round size, instrument, timing, use of proceeds, data room link.

**Gather this first**  
Term target, use of proceeds split, link to folder.

**Build steps**  
1) Big ask line.  
2) Short use of proceeds.  
3) Clear contact and link.

**Worked example**  
“Raising £3.2M Seed. Use: 55 percent team, 25 percent UA after gates, 20 percent services and infra.”

# Series A Deck Tutorial (15 slides)

Use Seed as a base. Replace or extend as follows.

## 1. Cover with current state  
Add installs, MAU, revenue run rate, top geos. Keep the in-engine image.

## 2. Progress since Seed  
Timeline of experiments. For each win, show the evidence chart and what changed. Include one thing you stopped and why.

## 3. Product deep dive  
Show live-ops cadence per week or month, top sinks and sources, and economy stability. Add a chart of ARPPU stability.

## 4. Traction overview  
D1, D7, D30, payer rate, ARPDAU, refunds. Split by platform if different. Label dates and units.

## 5. Cohorts and retention  
Lines by install week and platform. No blended curves. One annotation for each material change.

## 6. Monetisation deep dive  
SKU ladder, first purchase funnel, price tests, ad stack, refunds trend. Add one actionable insight.

## 7. Unit economics  
LTV by cohort and channel, CAC by channel, LTV to CAC ratios, payback windows with a ± 20 percent band. Note attribution method and fee assumptions.

## 8. Growth engine  
Channel mix, creative taxonomy, volume and win rate, refresh cadence, creator programmes. Include one winning creative and why it won.

## 9. Tech and operations

New markets, content cadence, infra scale plan. Tie each to staff or vendors.

## 11. Team for scale  
Recent hires and open roles mapped to risks. Two lines per person with shipped outcomes.

## 12. Financials  
Revenue drivers, gross margin drivers, burn, runway, scenarios. Link to budget workbook.

## 13. EU distribution and fees impact  
Margin bridge from gross to net by platform based on your choices. Add date and source notes.

## 14. Risks and mitigations  
Five named risks with owners and next checkpoints. Short, testable mitigations.

## 15. The ask  
Round size, timing, tranche plan, data room link.

# Due Diligence Overview Deck

## Structure and links
1) Executive summary with links to folders.  
2) Legal and company structure.  
3) Cap table and options.  
4) IP and contractor assignments.  
5) Product docs and roadmap index.  
6) Live-ops calendars and release process.  
7) Data schema and event dictionary.  
8) Cohort exports by geo, source, platform, version.  
9) Attribution method and windows per platform.  
10) UA logs and creative taxonomy with results.  
11) Community and creator programmes.  
12) Financial history and forecasts.  
13) Budget by function and vendor schedules.  
14) UA spend logs and reconciliation.  
15) Technology and security.  
16) Compliance, Online Safety Act posture if UGC or chat, age assurance if relevant.  
17) Risk register with owners and residual risk.  
18) Appendix index.

Each slide contains the summary, the link, and a short “how to read this” note.

---

# Visual and Fidelity Guide

**Foundations**  
16:9, 1920x1080, safe margins 40 px, single sans-serif family, Title 40 pt or larger, Heading 28 pt or larger, Body 24 pt or larger, Labels 16 pt or larger, contrast at least WCAG AA.

**Imagery and video**  
Gameplay only, 1080p, H.264, 15 MB or less. Keep HUD if it helps.

**Charts**  
Bars, lines, waterfalls, funnels, cohort curves. Label axes, units, currency, and dates. Avoid pies. One annotated insight on the chart.

**Tables**  
Six rows by four columns or fewer in core deck. Larger tables move to appendix.

**Motion**  
Simple appear or fade. Export to static PDF for send-ahead.

**Brand discipline**  
Title top left, source bottom right, slide number bottom centre. No icons.

**File hygiene**  
Master template only. Version naming “DeckName_vMajor.Minor_Date”. Keep a change log slide. PDF size 12 to 15 MB or less.

---

# Tutorials and Worked Examples

## A) LTV and payback
**Inputs**  
Cohort size, cumulative revenue per day, CAC per channel.

**Steps**  
1) LTV at day n equals cumulative revenue by day n divided by cohort size.  
2) Payback day is the first day where LTV is greater than or equal to CAC.  
3) Repeat per channel. Draw lines with bands.

**Mini example**  
Cohort 1,000 users. Cumulative revenue day 30 is £520. LTV D30 equals £0.52. If CAC is £0.40 you have payback by day 30. If CAC is £0.70 you do not. Plan your next tests from there.

## B) Simple cohort chart
Install weeks on the x axis. Percent active on the y axis. One line per week. Label dates. Add annotations at test weeks.

## C) EU fee margin bridge
Start with gross revenue by platform. Subtract fee lines for your chosen routes. Subtract refunds and taxes if used. End at net. Repeat by platform then show a combined total. Put the date of the policy and a source note.

---

# Platform Annex with KPI Gates and Experiment Template

**How to use**  
Run 2 to 4 week tests. Define pass or iterate gates before you start. Measure only what you can act on.

**Experiment template**  
Objective, audience and geo, entry points, tracking plan, creative set, budget cap, pass gate, next action.

**Telegram Mini Apps**  
Start rate, FTUE, D1, first purchase or trial attach, refunds, simple viral K. Example pass gate for an alpha run: Start 60 percent or higher, FTUE 70 percent or higher, D1 30 percent or higher, first purchase 1.0 percent or higher, K 0.15 or higher, refunds under 2 percent.

**Poki web**  
Capsule CTR, conversion to play, time to first action, first session median minutes, rewarded acceptance at scale. Example pass gate: conversion to play 70 percent or higher, first session median 6 to 10 minutes by genre, time to first interaction 3 seconds or less.

**iOS**  
D1 35 percent or higher, D7 12 percent or higher, tutorial completion 80 percent or higher, first purchase or trial attach as stated, ASA Day 30 ROAS proxy near target. Compliance clean.

**Android**  
D1 35 percent or higher, D7 12 percent or higher, first purchase 1.2 percent or higher or trials 2.0 percent or higher, Day 7 ROAS proxy near 40 percent of target, store A B uplift 10 percent or higher.

**Steam**  
Wishlist velocity, demo to wishlist rate, launch wishlist to sale multiple, early review score and count. Example pass gate: demo to wishlist 25 percent or higher, early reviews 85 percent positive with 500 or more reviews by day 14.

---

# Evidence Table Template

**Columns**  
Claim text, slide number, source title and link, source type and date, method notes or caveats, confidence 1 to 5, owner, next refresh date.

**How to keep it current**  
Add the date you last checked each policy or fee. Schedule refresh for anything older than 90 days.

---

# Checklists and Rubrics

**One minute skim**  
Title states the takeaway. One primary graphic. Units and dates on every chart.

**Readability**  
Body 24 pt, labels 16 pt, one idea per slide.

**Completeness by stage**  
Seed: problem, solution, market, proof or model, GTM tests, team, ask.  
Series A: traction, cohorts, unit economics, growth engine, scale plan, risks, ask.  
Diligence: full index with links.

**Acceptance rubric 0 to 5**  
5 investor ready, 4 minor tweaks, 3 needs revision, 2 major issues, 1 unusable.

---

# Glossary
TAM, SAM, SOM: total, served, reachable market.  
D1, D7, D30: percent of a cohort active on day 1, 7, 30.  
ARPDAU: average revenue per daily active user.  
ARPPU: average revenue per paying user.  
LTV: lifetime value to a stated day.  
CAC: cost to acquire one user.  
Payback: first day where LTV is greater than or equal to CAC.  
FTUE: first time user experience.  
UA: user acquisition.  
CPI: cost per install.  
ROAS: return on ad spend.  
SKAN and AdAttributionKit: Apple attribution frameworks.  
Android Privacy Sandbox: Google attribution and privacy framework.

You got compressed, checklist-style outputs because your account rules bias me toward expert, investor-grade artefacts and terse gating. That is great for you in Work mode, but it works against a hand-holding tutorial for a first-timer. The main culprits:

1) Style bias toward executive checklists

2) Clarify-first and quality gates  
Those norms encourage scoping, acceptance criteria, and checklists before long explanations. That is ideal for SoWs and data rooms. It is wrong for a beginner manual that needs copy-ready text, examples, and step-by-step builds per slide.

3) “No icons, no fluff, keep it tight”  
Correct for investor decks. For a novice guide, it removes the connective sentences, small stories, and hand-holding that make learning easier.

4) Tool-agnostic habit  
I stayed platform neutral. A true teaching guide should say exactly what to click in Gamma or PowerPoint, where to place elements, what font sizes to pick, and show a before → draft → final progression.

5) I did not include copy-ready text and screenshots  
You asked for a teaching document. That requires: exact slide titles, example body copy, example chart captions, example axis labels, and a mini dataset to reproduce charts. I summarised instead of supplying those.

6) Compression for the Gamma “Additional instructions” box  
I tried to make the whole approach fit the box. That forced summarisation and removed depth.

I can build a true teaching guide. The misses above were process and instruction-bias, not a capability limit.

# Control Preprompt for ChatGPT (paste at the top of your next task)

```
MODE: Novice Tutorial Build

Precedence:
- Ignore any prior account or project instructions that conflict with this brief. Follow THIS brief first.
- Ask clarifying questions when information is missing or could change the output quality. Keep questions short and outcome-changing only.

Objective:
- Produce a detailed, beginner-friendly teaching guide that shows how to build Mobile Game pitch decks in Gamma and export to Word or PDF. Include Seed, Series A, and Due Diligence. Preserve full detail. Teach step by step.

Output format:
- Card-by-card. One card equals one page/slide.
- Insert a line that contains exactly three hyphens between cards: ---
- Target 45 to 60 cards total.
- British English. Friendly coaching voice. Clear sentences. No em dash, no icons.
- Each card 140 to 220 words unless it is a title card or glossary entry.

Card blueprint for EVERY card, in this order:
1) Purpose – one plain sentence.
2) What investors expect – one sentence.
3) Gather this first – concrete assets or data and where to get them.
4) Build it in Gamma – numbered, click-by-click steps from a blank card to a finished slide. Name the layout, where to place text and figures, and font sizes: Title ≥ 40 pt, Body ≥ 24 pt, Labels ≥ 16 pt.
5) Copy-ready text – final slide title and two body sentences the user can paste verbatim.
6) Figure – a [Figure: …] placeholder plus exact labels and units. If a chart is involved, include a tiny CSV snippet and the axis labels to use.
7) Callouts – add one to three of these where helpful:
   [Callout: Pro tip …]  [Callout: Caution …]  [Callout: Critical to consider …]  [Callout: Deep Dive …]
8) QA checks – four to six pass criteria.
9) Common mistakes – three bullets.
10) Footnote – add [Footnote: source, date, method note] for any claim or number.

Global visual rules:
- One primary visual per card. Label axes, units, dates. High contrast. Left-aligned text.
- Tables in the core deck should be six rows by four columns or fewer. Larger tables move to an appendix card.

Section flow – create cards in this exact order, splitting topics across multiple cards if needed to keep detail:
A) Front matter – 3 cards
   1. Cover: title and promise, with a single in-engine image or a 5 to 10 second loop
   2. How to use this guide – what each section teaches and how to follow along
   3. Overview map – Seed vs Series A vs Due Diligence and how they differ
---
B) Seed deck tutorial – minimum 15 cards
   1. Title and promise
   2. Why now – timing drivers
   3. Market size and reachable SOM – bottom-up example with mini CSV
   4. Player problem and desire – quotes or clip guidance
   5. Fifteen-second hook – capture spec and captioning
   6. Core loop and two systems – diagram steps
   7. Distribution advantage – table plus channel rationale
   8. Monetisation plan – SKU ladder and first purchase path
   9. Early metrics OR LTV model with sensitivity – mini dataset and formula
   10. Go-to-market for 180 days – experiment calendar with gates
   11. Competitive landscape and your wedge – simple 2 by 2 or table
   12. Team fit – coverage map and two-line bios
   13. Milestones and KPI gates – timeline with exits
   14. Financial snapshot – burn line and unit targets
   15. The ask – round, use of proceeds, data room link
   + Add “Checklist: Seed readiness” and “Self-test: move to Series A”
---
C) Series A deck tutorial – minimum 15 cards
   Mirror Seed, but upgrade each topic with live data:
   1. Title with current state – installs, MAU, revenue run rate
   2. Progress since Seed – experiment wins and kills
   3. Product deep dive – live-ops cadence and economy stability
   4. Traction overview – D1, D7, D30, payer rate, ARPDAU, refunds
   5. Cohorts and retention – by platform, no blends, mini CSV
   6. Monetisation deep dive – SKU ladder, first purchase funnel, ad stack
   7. Unit economics – LTV by cohort and channel, CAC, payback with bands
   8. Growth engine – channel mix, creative taxonomy, creator programmes
   9. Tech and operations – pipeline, analytics, compliance
   10. Scale roadmap – markets, content cadence, infra
   11. Team for scale – hires and open roles mapped to risks
   12. Financials – drivers, burn, runway, scenarios
   13. EU distribution and fees impact – margin bridge example
   14. Risks and mitigations – owners and next checkpoints
   15. The ask – round size, tranche plan, links
   + Add “Checklist: Series A readiness” and “Self-test: proceed to diligence”
---
D) Due Diligence overview – 10 to 18 cards
   Executive summary with deep links; legal and company structure; cap table and options;
   IP and contractor assignments; product docs and roadmap index; live-ops calendars and release process;
   data schema and event dictionary; cohort exports and method notes; attribution method per platform;
   UA logs and creative taxonomy with results; community and creator programmes; financial history and forecasts;
   budget by function and vendor schedules; UA spend logs and reconciliation; technology and security;
   compliance posture; risk register with owners; appendix index. Each card includes a short “how to read this” note and a Footnote where policy appears.
---
E) Tutorials and worked examples – 6 to 10 cards
   LTV and payback step-by-step; cohort chart build; EU fee margin bridge with a simple waterfall;
   experiment design template; “build this slide” recipes for loop, store ladder, and timeline;
   evidence table template with example entries.
---
F) Platform annex with KPI gates – 5 cards
   One card each for Telegram Mini Apps, Poki, iOS, Android, Steam. For each: entry points, test windows,
   KPI gates, compliance notes, and an example experiment.
---
G) Glossary – 2 to 4 cards
   Define TAM, SAM, SOM, D1, D7, D30, ARPDAU, ARPPU, LTV, CAC, payback, FTUE, UA, CPI, ROAS, SKAN, AdAttributionKit, Android Privacy Sandbox.
---
H) Final checklists and rubrics – 2 to 3 cards
   One-minute skim, readability and completeness; acceptance rubric 0 to 5 with examples of what earns a 5;
   versioning and change log instructions.

Data and figures:
- Any chart card must include a mini CSV and the exact axis labels, units, and date range. Show one worked calculation where helpful.
- Add a caption that names the method or attribution used.

Clarifying questions to ask up front:
1) Which platforms are in scope right now.
2) Do you have early metrics or should we teach with a model first.
3) Brand font or colour constraints for the theme.
4) Example datasets in GBP or USD.
5) Will gameplay video be embedded in Gamma or linked externally.

If no answers are given, proceed with sensible defaults and label them as placeholders.

- Ask clarifying questions when information is missing or could change output quality. Keep questions short and outcome-changing only.

Objective:
- Produce a detailed, beginner-friendly teaching guide that shows how to build Mobile Game pitch decks and export the guide to Word or PDF. Include Seed, Series A, and Due Diligence. Preserve full detail. Teach step by step.

Format of THIS guide:
- Card-by-card. One card equals one page.
- Insert a line with exactly three hyphens between cards to create page breaks in the guide: ---
- Target 45 to 60 cards total.
- British English. Friendly coaching voice. Clear sentences. No em dash, no icons.
- Each card 140 to 220 words unless it is a title card or glossary entry.

Card blueprint for EVERY card, in this order:
1) Purpose – one plain sentence.
2) What investors expect – one sentence.
3) Gather this first – concrete assets or data and where to get them.
4) Build it in your slide tool – numbered, click-by-click steps from a blank slide to a finished slide in PowerPoint, Keynote, or Google Slides. Name the layout, where to place text and figures, and font sizes: Title ≥ 40 pt, Body ≥ 24 pt, Labels ≥ 16 pt.
5) Copy-ready text – final slide title and two body sentences the user can paste verbatim.
6) Figure – a [Figure: …] placeholder plus exact labels and units. If a chart is involved, include a tiny CSV snippet and the axis labels to use.
7) Callouts – add one to three where helpful:
   [Callout: Pro tip …]  [Callout: Caution …]  [Callout: Critical to consider …]  [Callout: Deep Dive …]
8) QA checks – four to six pass criteria.
9) Common mistakes – three bullets.
10) Footnote – add [Footnote: source, date, method note] for any claim or number.

Global visual rules:
- One primary visual per slide. Label axes, units, dates. High contrast. Left-aligned text.
- Tables in the core deck should be six rows by four columns or fewer; larger tables move to an appendix slide.

Section flow for the guide – create cards in this exact order, splitting topics across multiple cards if needed to keep detail:

A) Front matter – 3 cards
1. Cover: title and promise, with a single in-engine image or a 5 to 10 second loop
2. How to use this guide – what each section teaches and how to follow along
3. Overview map – Seed vs Series A vs Due Diligence and how they differ
---
B) Seed deck tutorial – minimum 15 cards
1. Title and promise
2. Why now – timing drivers
3. Market size and reachable SOM – bottom-up example with mini CSV
4. Player problem and desire – quotes or clip guidance
5. Fifteen-second hook – capture spec and captioning
6. Core loop and two systems – diagram steps
7. Distribution advantage – table plus channel rationale
8. Monetisation plan – SKU ladder and first purchase path
9. Early metrics OR LTV model with sensitivity – mini dataset and formula
10. Go-to-market for 180 days – experiment calendar with gates
11. Competitive landscape and your wedge – simple 2 by 2 or table
12. Team fit – coverage map and two-line bios
13. Milestones and KPI gates – timeline with exits
14. Financial snapshot – burn line and unit targets
15. The ask – round, use of proceeds, data room link
+ Add “Checklist: Seed readiness” and “Self-test: move to Series A”
---
C) Series A deck tutorial – minimum 15 cards
Mirror Seed, but upgrade each topic with live data:
1. Title with current state – installs, MAU, revenue run rate
2. Progress since Seed – experiment wins and kills
3. Product deep dive – live-ops cadence and economy stability
4. Traction overview – D1, D7, D30, payer rate, ARPDAU, refunds
5. Cohorts and retention – by platform, no blends, mini CSV
6. Monetisation deep dive – SKU ladder, first purchase funnel, ad stack
7. Unit economics – LTV by cohort and channel, CAC, payback with bands
8. Growth engine – channel mix, creative taxonomy, creator programmes
9. Tech and operations – pipeline, analytics, compliance
10. Scale roadmap – markets, content cadence, infra
11. Team for scale – hires and open roles mapped to risks
12. Financials – drivers, burn, runway, scenarios
13. EU distribution and fees impact – margin bridge example
14. Risks and mitigations – owners and next checkpoints
15. The ask – round size, tranche plan, links
+ Add “Checklist: Series A readiness” and “Self-test: proceed to diligence”
---
D) Due Diligence overview – 10 to 18 cards
Executive summary with deep links; legal and company structure; cap table and options; IP and contractor assignments; product docs and roadmap index; live-ops calendars and release process; data schema and event dictionary; cohort exports and method notes; attribution method per platform; UA logs and creative taxonomy with results; community and creator programmes; financial history and forecasts; budget by function and vendor schedules; UA spend logs and reconciliation; technology and security; compliance posture; risk register with owners; appendix index. Each card includes a short “how to read this” note and a Footnote where policy appears.
---
E) Tutorials and worked examples – 6 to 10 cards
LTV and payback step-by-step; cohort chart build; EU fee margin bridge with a simple waterfall; experiment design template; “build this slide” recipes for loop, store ladder, and timeline; evidence table template with example entries.
---
F) Platform annex with KPI gates – 5 cards
One card each for Telegram Mini Apps, Poki, iOS, Android, Steam. For each: entry points, test windows, KPI gates, compliance notes, and an example experiment.
---
G) Glossary – 2 to 4 cards
Define TAM, SAM, SOM, D1, D7, D30, ARPDAU, ARPPU, LTV, CAC, payback, FTUE, UA, CPI, ROAS, SKAN, AdAttributionKit, Android Privacy Sandbox.
---
H) Final checklists and rubrics – 2 to 3 cards
One-minute skim, readability and completeness; acceptance rubric 0 to 5 with examples of what earns a 5; versioning and change log instructions.

Data and figures:
- Any chart card must include a mini CSV and the exact axis labels, units, and date range. Show one worked calculation where helpful.
- Add a caption that names the method or attribution used.

Clarifying questions to ask up front:
1) Which platforms are in scope right now.
2) Do you have early metrics or should we teach with a model first.
3) Brand font or colour constraints for the theme.
4) Example datasets in GBP or USD.
5) Will gameplay video be embedded in the guide or linked externally.

If no answers are given, proceed with sensible defaults and label them as placeholders.

## A2. How to use this guide

1) Purpose  
Show you how to learn, decide, and build slides that investors can understand in seconds.

2) What investors expect  
A clear claim per slide, a visual that proves it, and a short line that tells them what to conclude.

3) Gather this first  
A working slide tool, a simple neutral theme, a folder for images and mock screenshots, and a blank spreadsheet where you will paste the tiny example datasets from this guide.

4) Build it in your slide tool  
Use a text-first layout. Add three short sections: Learn, Decide, Build. Leave space to paste a screenshot of your finished slide later.

5) Copy-ready text  
Title: How to use this guide  
Body: Read each slide tutorial in three passes. First, understand the **job** of the slide and **why it matters** to an investor. Second, apply the **expert lens** before you open your tool:  
• Necessity: does each element directly support the claim.  
• Sufficiency: is there enough proof for a sceptic to agree.  
• Comparability: are units, dates, and cohorts comparable.  
• Traceability: can a reader see where the number came from.  
Third, build with the steps, then run the **QA checks** and fix issues listed under **Common mistakes**. Write the one-line takeaway under the visual first, then the title. Replace example datasets later with your real numbers and add a dated source line.  
Caption: Learn the reasoning, then copy the layout.

6) Figure  
[Figure: Learning loop diagram. Labels: Job → Expert lens → Build → QA → Revise.]

7) Callouts  
[Callout: Critical to consider One slide, one claim. If you have two claims, make two slides.]  
[Callout: Pro tip Aim for time-to-clarity under three seconds. Title plus visual should carry the message without the presenter.]  
[Callout: Deep Dive Use the tiny CSVs to practise charts. Swap with your data only when your layout is already clear.]

8) QA checks  
A single claim is obvious. Visual and takeaway agree. Units, dates, and axes are labelled. A footnote names the source or states “example dataset.” A colleague can state the slide’s point after a five second skim.

9) Common mistakes  
Slogan titles without a takeaway line. Mixed units or undated figures. Busy slides with more than one visual fighting for attention.

10) Footnote  
[Footnote: Examples use USD and GBP for teaching. Replace with your currency and include the source name and date.]

# Verdict
- Overall: not yet award-winning for novices.
- For experienced founders: high potential once tone is softened and the “why” is made explicit.

# Scorecard (1–10)
- Teaching tone for novices: 6  
- Clarity of purpose per slide: 7  
- Depth of explanation and “why it matters”: 6  
- Visual fidelity guidance: 7  
- Platform neutrality without vagueness: 8  
- Cohesion across sections: 7  
- Reader engagement and retention: 6  
- Fitness for a ≤60 page limit: 7

# What is working
1) Structure is solid: Seed, Series A, Diligence, plus tutorials and platform annex.  
2) Visual QA is present: sizes, labels, currency units, captions, footnotes.  
3) Platform agnostic and dual currency are handled cleanly.  
4) Inclusion of datasets and figure specs is a good teaching foundation.

# What is missing for award-winning quality
1) Learner guidance, not just construction steps. The guide tells the reader what to place, but rarely explains why an investor needs that proof at that moment, how the slide supports the deck’s narrative, or how to recognise when the slide is “good enough.”  
2) Worked reasoning beside each step. Novices need a short paragraph that shows the decision logic: if X, then choose layout A; if Y, choose layout B, with a one-sentence rationale.  
3) Before → draft → final exemplars. A single exemplar per tricky slide type is the fastest way to teach.  
4) Takeaway lines that model the message. Every slide should include a one-line conclusion placed under the visual. The guide needs to model that conclusion text, not only the layout.  
5) Transitional coaching. Add two or three sentences at the end of each tutorial card that say what the investor is now primed to see next, so the reader understands flow, not just slides.  
6) Cognitive scaffolding. Add named lenses a novice can reuse: necessity, sufficiency, comparability, and traceability. Use them as little checklists within the prose.  
7) Voice. The prose is still terse and mechanical in places. Replace list fragments with short, instructive sentences. Keep bullets only where true checklists or gates are needed.

# Concrete fixes to reach award level
Use these five upgrades throughout, then we can re-audit.

1) Add an “Investor lens” paragraph on every slide tutorial  
Two to three sentences that state: what proof this slide delivers, what conclusion the investor should reach in three seconds, and how this advances the story.

2) Add a “Why this layout” paragraph on every slide tutorial  
Two sentences that explain why the chosen layout matches the claim. Example: “Use a single line chart here because the point is trajectory, not absolute size. A bar chart would hide the early slope and weaken the claim.”

3) Add a model “Takeaway line” on every slide  
One sentence that appears below the figure. Example: “Day-90 LTV is £1.20 and $1.49, which clears our £0.90 and $1.10 CAC on two channels.”

4) Include one before → draft → final micro-example per difficult slide type  
Do this for Market size and SOM, LTV and payback, Cohort retention, EU fee margin bridge, and Go-to-market timeline with gates. Each example gets a sentence on what improved and why.

5) Replace mechanical list phrasing with coaching sentences  
Target two to three short sentences per sub-section where you currently have fragments. Keep bullets only for QA checks and common mistakes.

# Page budget to stay under 60
You can reach award-level depth without breaking the limit by tightening counts per section.

- A) Front matter: 3 cards  
- B) Seed tutorial: 14 cards  
- C) Series A tutorial: 14 cards  
- D) Diligence overview: 10 cards  
- E) Tutorials and worked examples: 7 cards  
- F) Platform annex with KPI gates: 5 cards  
- G) Glossary: 3 cards  
- H) Final checklists and rubrics: 2 cards  
Total: 58 cards

# Example of the improved tone and depth
Below is one reworked tutorial to show the voice and teaching style that earns higher marks.

## B3. Seed: Market size and reachable SOM

Purpose  
Show the size of the opportunity, then prove a small, believable slice you can reach first.

Investor lens  
Investors decide whether your SOM is real in under ten seconds. They look for dates, units, and a bottom-up path tied to channels and pricing. If SOM is a flat percent of TAM, they will discount the slide immediately.

Gather this first  
One recent, named estimate for TAM and SAM with dates. Your first two channels and a starting LTV or pricing assumption. If you have no data, use the example CSV here to learn the layout.

Build it in your slide tool  
Start with three vertical bars labelled TAM, SAM, SOM. Label units and dates on the bars, not in a legend, so they can be read at a glance. Place a one-sentence bottom-up explanation directly under the bars. Keep the SOM bar a different shade to cue “this is ours.”

Copy-ready text  
Title: Market size and our reachable slice  
Body: We begin in English markets on two platforms. With creator discovery and search ads, a first-year reachable audience of 600k users at a £12 or $15 LTV yields a £7.2m or $9.0m SOM.

Figure  
[Figure: Three-bar chart. X: TAM, SAM, SOM. Y: £ billions and $ billions. SOM labelled in both currencies.]  
Mini CSV:
```
Category,ValueGBP,ValueUSD,Unit,Date
TAM,3500,4400,£m/$m,2024
SAM,900,1130,£m/$m,2025
SOM,7.2,9.0,£m/$m,Year 1 model
```

Takeaway line  
Our first-year reachable slice is £7.2m or $9.0m based on channels we can execute now.

QA checks  
Dates appear on all bars. SOM has a bottom-up sentence. Units are consistent. No legend required at 100 percent zoom.

Common mistakes  
Picking a flat percent of TAM. Mixing gross and net. Undated sources.

Footnote  
Name the market source and date. If you used the example CSV, say “example values for layout only.”

Transition  
Next, we explain the player problem and desire that make this slice winnable at a sane cost.

# Acceptance bar for award-winning tone
A section passes when:  
- Every tutorial includes an Investor lens paragraph, a Why this layout paragraph, and a model Takeaway line.  
- At least three hard slides include a before → draft → final exemplar with one sentence of improvement rationale.  
- Every figure has visible units, dates, and a caption that states the method.  
- The narrative reads smoothly in prose, with bullets reserved for QA and mistakes only.  
- Total length remains at or under 60 pages.

## A2. How to use this guide

1) Purpose  
Show how to learn, decide, and build slides that investors can understand in seconds.

2) What investors expect  
Evidence that is necessary, sufficient, comparable, and traceable.

3) Gather this first  
Neutral theme, image assets, a blank spreadsheet for example CSVs, and a working list of acronyms you will define at first use.

4) Build it in your slide tool  
Use a text-first layout. Add three paragraphs titled Learn, Decide, Build. Leave a placeholder area where you will paste a screenshot of your own finished slide later.

5) Copy-ready text  
Title: How to use this guide  
Body: Read each tutorial in three passes. First, understand the **job** of the slide and why an investor needs that proof now. Second, use the **investor lens** before you open your tool: is every element necessary for the claim, is the proof sufficient for a sceptic, are units and dates comparable, and can the source be traced. Third, build with the steps, then run the QA checks and fix the listed mistakes. Write the one-line takeaway beneath the visual first, then write the title. Use the tiny CSVs to practise charts, and only swap in your numbers when the layout already communicates clearly.  
Caption: Learn the reasoning first, then copy the layout.

6) Figure  
[Figure: Learning loop diagram. Labels: Job → Investor lens → Build → QA → Revise.]

7) Callouts  
[Callout: Critical to consider One slide, one claim. Make two slides if there are two claims.]  
[Callout: Pro tip Aim for time-to-clarity under three seconds.]

8) QA checks  
Single claim is obvious. Visual and takeaway agree. Axes, units, and dates are labelled. Footnote names the source or states “example dataset.”

9) Common mistakes  
Slogan titles without a takeaway line. Mixed units or undated figures. Two competing visuals on one slide.

10) Footnote  
[Footnote: Replace example captions with your source name and date.]

It is not a brand manual or a legal pack. Reading time: 45 to 60 minutes. Export your finished guide to Word or PDF from File → Export.

## E1. Tutorial: calculate LTV and payback step by step

0) Slide goal  
Teach a beginner how to compute LTV and payback from raw cohort data.

1) Purpose  
Walk through the maths with a tiny dataset so you can reproduce the chart in your own deck. Why this matters: LTV and payback are decision metrics and investors will ask how you got them.

2) What investors expect  
Clear formulas, day ranges, net revenue, attribution window, and a payback result per channel.

3) Gather this first  
Cohort revenue per user by day, CAC by channel, refund rate, attribution method and window.

4) Build it in your slide tool  
Create a two-column slide. Left: steps 1 to 4 with short formulas. Right: a small table of inputs and a final payback result. Title 40 pt. Labels 16 pt. Add a method caption.

5) Copy-ready text  
Title: How to compute LTV and payback  
Body: LTV is the cumulative net revenue per user for a cohort. Payback is the day LTV crosses CAC for a channel. Use net revenue after platform fees, payments, and VAT.

6) Figure  
[Figure: Small LTV table and payback result.]

Mini CSV  
```
Day,RevPU_Net_GBP,RevPU_Net_USD
30,0.70,0.88
60,1.10,1.38
90,1.45,1.82
```
CAC by channel: Search £1.20 or $1.50; Creator £1.05 or $1.30.

7) Callouts  
[Callout: Pro tip Plot LTV cumulative. Daily swings smooth out.]  
[Callout: Caution State attribution method and window.]

8) QA checks  
Net not gross, CAC per channel stated, day scale shown, refund handling noted.

9) Common mistakes  
Blending channels, mixing net and gross, unlabeled currency.

10) Footnote  
[Footnote: Attribution method and window stated in the caption.]

5) Copy-ready text  
Title: Write experiments that make decisions easy  
Body: Example: Tutorial B. Gate D1 ≥ 30 percent. Duration one full weekly cohort per platform. Decision rule: Advance if both platforms pass; otherwise iterate and retest.

Title: Telegram Mini Apps: how to test responsibly  
Body: Entry points: bot menu, deep links from channels, pinned messages in groups. Early gates: CTR from deep link ≥ 3 percent, first session 5 to 7 minutes, D1 ≥ 25 percent. Test window 7 to 14 days with one cohort. Decision: advance when two consecutive cohorts pass D1 and session targets.

```
Metric,Gate
CTR_DeepLink,≥ 3%
FirstSession,5–7 min

## H1. Final checklists: one-minute skim, readability, completeness

0) Slide goal

2) What investors expect  
One claim per slide, a visible takeaway line, dated visuals.

3) Gather this first  
The final deck, a colleague, and a timer.

4) Build it in your slide tool  
Checklist with ten items and tick boxes. Include a skim test and a print test.

5) Copy-ready text  
Title: One-minute skim and completeness checks  
Body: The title states the claim. A one-line takeaway sits under each visual. All charts have units, axes, and dates. Methods appear in captions. Platforms and channels are not blended. Money is in £ and $. The ask has a split that sums to 100 percent. File name has version and date. Skim test passes in one minute. A4 print at 100 percent is readable.

9) Common mistakes  
Crowded slides, blended cohorts, missing captions.

## C7. Unit economics: LTV by cohort, CAC, payback bands

## A2. How to use this guide

0) Slide goal  
Show a first-time builder how to work through the cards step by step.

1) Purpose  
Explain the workflow from reading a card to completing a polished slide. Why this matters: a repeatable process reduces stress and rework.

2) What investors expect  
A short, clear deck that reads in four minutes and proves claims with dated figures.

3) Gather this first  
A blank presentation, one brand colour and font, a “Figures” folder, and a simple file naming pattern like Game_Pitch_v1.0_YYYY-MM-DD.

4) Build it in your slide tool  
Make a two-column slide. Left column lists the ten blocks every card contains. Right column shows the loop: Read the card, Gather assets, Build the slide, Write a one-line takeaway under the visual, Add a method caption, Run QA checks, Tick the box.

5) Copy-ready text  
Title: How to use these cards  
Body: Read one card, gather what it asks for, and build the slide exactly once. Write your one-line takeaway under the visual, then title the slide. Add units, dates, and a short method caption. Run the QA checks before moving on.

6) Figure  
[Figure: Flow diagram. Labels: Read → Gather → Build → Caption → QA → Tick.]

7) Callouts  
[Callout: Pro tip Keep this guide open on one screen and your deck on another.]  
[Callout: Caution Do not skip captions. Methods and dates live there.]

8) QA checks  
Every slide has units, dates, and a one-line takeaway. Captions present. File name dated.

9) Common mistakes  
Building three slides at once, forgetting the caption, tiny text.

10) Footnote  
[Footnote: This is a teaching guide. Do not send it with your investor deck.]

---

## A3. Overview map: Seed vs Series A vs Due Diligence

Show what changes at each raise stage so the builder knows what to emphasise.

1) Purpose

2) What investors expect  
Seed: sharp problem, loop, market, wedge, plan, ask. Series A: cohorts, LTV and CAC, operations, risks, ask. Diligence: indexed proof of all claims.

3) Gather this first  
Your current stage, any early metrics, and a list of proofs you can show.

4) Build it in your slide tool  
Create a three-column table labelled Seed, Series A, Diligence. Put five to six bullets under each that summarise what must be present.

5) Copy-ready text  
Title: What changes by stage  
Body: Seed proves the plan with early signals. Series A proves repeatable traction with clean economics. Diligence lets an investor verify numbers, ownership, and operations without hunting.

6) Figure  
[Figure: Three-column table. Labels: Seed, Series A, Diligence.]

[ASSISTANT]
# This script compiles the complete "Mobile Game Pitch Deck Guide (Novice, 2025)" 
# into a Word document with page breaks between cards.
# It assembles Sections A–H, including the newly completed A2, A3, and Section B,
# plus previously delivered Sections C–H content in this chat.
#
# If python-docx is unavailable, this will fail. In that case, we would retry using a .pdf fallback.
from docx import Document
from docx.shared import Pt, Inches
from docx.enum.text import WD_ALIGN_PARAGRAPH
from docx.oxml.ns import qn
from docx.oxml import OxmlElement

doc = Document()

def add_heading(text, level=1):
    p = doc.add_paragraph()
    run = p.add_run(text)
    if level == 1:
        run.font.size = Pt(18)
        run.bold = True
    elif level == 2:
        run.font.size = Pt(16)
        run.bold = True
    else:
        run.font.size = Pt(14)
        run.bold = True
    return p

def add_para(text, size=12, bold=False):
    p = doc.add_paragraph()
    run = p.add_run(text)
    run.font.size = Pt(size)
    run.bold = bold
    return p

def add_break():
    # Add visible '---' then a Word page break
    add_para('---', size=10)
    p = doc.add_paragraph()
    run = p.add_run()
    run.add_break()  # line break
    # Insert a page break
    run.add_break(docx.text.run.WD_BREAK.PAGE)

6) Figure  
[Figure: Channel table.]

Mini CSV  
```
Channel,Why,Gate
Search,Match to intent,D1 ≥ 30%
Creator,High watch time,CTR ≥ 1.5%
Community,Trust and depth,Session ≥ 6 min
```

7) Callouts  
[Callout: Pro tip Start narrow and expand once a channel passes gates twice.]  
[Callout: Caution Do not blend channels in early reporting.]

8) QA checks  
Clear gates, short rationale, test windows in caption.

9) Common mistakes  
Too many channels, no gates, no attribution method.

10) Footnote  
[Footnote: Attribution method and window appear in the caption.]

# B8. Monetisation plan: SKU ladder and first purchase

1) Purpose

SKU names, benefits, prices, and trigger timing.

Title: First purchase in two short sessions  
Body: The first offer appears after a real value moment, and the benefit is specific. Prices show both currencies.

Starter,2.99,3.49,Unlock event track
Bundle S,4.99,5.99,Speed upgrades
Pass,9.99,11.99,Exclusive rewards
```

7) Callouts  
[Callout: Pro tip Put the first offer no sooner than the second meaningful win.]  
[Callout: Caution Avoid pay-to-win optics.]

8) QA checks  
Dual currency, specific benefits, path timing stated.

9) Common mistakes  
Price-only list, six or more SKUs, vague benefits.

10) Footnote  
[Footnote: Replace placeholders with your store when live.]

# B9. Early metrics or LTV model with sensitivity

1) Purpose  
Show either honest early metrics or a simple model that becomes your benchmark.

3) Gather this first  
D1, D7, D30 if you have them, or a model with payer rate and ARPDAU that you will aim to beat.

4) Build it in your slide tool  
Create a small table. If modelling, add a sensitivity band of plus or minus 15 percent on payer rate.

5) Copy-ready text  
Title: Early proof or a clear starting model  
Body: We make targets explicit now so we can show progress later. Numbers are net and dated.

```
Metric,Value,Unit,Date

```

[Callout: Caution Never blend platforms.]

Dates present, net figures, definitions stated.

10) Footnote  
[Footnote: Extract date and attribution method in caption.]

# B10. Go-to-market for 180 days: experiment calendar with gates

# B11. Competitive landscape and your wedge

# B16. Checklist: Seed readiness

1) Purpose

Your near-final deck and a colleague for a skim test.

Checklist with ten items and tick boxes.

5) Copy-ready text  
Title: Seed deck readiness checklist  
Body: One claim per slide. Takeaway line under each figure. Dates and units on every chart. Methods in captions. Channels and platforms kept separate. Ask includes a split and proofs. File name shows version and date. Five-second skim test passed. A4 print at 100 percent is readable. Contrast passes WCAG AA.

6) Figure  
[Figure: Ten-item checklist.]

7) Callouts  
[Callout: Pro tip Do a laptop and tablet review at 100 percent zoom.]  
[Callout: Caution Remove internal comments.]

8) QA checks  
All boxes ticked. Version and date present. Methods visible.

9) Common mistakes  
Blended cohorts, missing captions, low-contrast charts.

10) Footnote  
[Footnote: Keep the checklist at the end of the deck.]

# B17. Self-test: move to Series A

1) Purpose  
Decide if you are ready to focus on proof, not just promise.

2) What investors expect  
Evidence of traction, unit economics, and safe operations.

3) Gather this first  
Two recent cohorts per platform, your LTV and CAC by channel, and a short uptime and policy status.

4) Build it in your slide tool  
Three yes or no questions with space for evidence and dates.

5) Copy-ready text  
Title: Self-test before you aim for Series A  
Body: Do two recent cohorts per platform reach your D1 and D7 targets. Do you have LTV and CAC by at least two channels with payback ≤ 120 days. Are the last 60 days of uptime and policy checks available.

6) Figure  
[Figure: Three-question card with evidence fields.]

7) Callouts  
[Callout: Pro tip If any answer is no, strengthen the Seed plan first.]  
[Callout: Caution Do not send diligence without current policy status.]

8) QA checks  
Evidence dated. Platforms split. Policy and uptime noted.

9) Common mistakes  
Anecdotes instead of data, no dates, blended cohorts.

10) Footnote  
[Footnote: Diligence index appears in Section D.]

[ASSISTANT]
# Create a Word document for Sections A1–A3 and B1–B17 of the
# "Mobile Game Pitch Deck Guide (Novice, 2025)" in an explanatory style.
#
# The user asked to finish A2, A3, and the full Seed section (B),
# then generate a Word doc quickly to avoid chat slowdowns.
#
# This script builds those sections only (A and B). We can append C–H later
# into the same file upon request.

from docx import Document
from docx.shared import Pt
from docx.enum.text import WD_BREAK

doc = Document()

def add_heading(text, size=18):
    p = doc.add_paragraph()
    run = p.add_run(text)
    run.bold = True
    run.font.size = Pt(size)

def add_block(label, content):
    p = doc.add_paragraph()
    run = p.add_run(f"{label} ")
    run.bold = True
    run.font.size = Pt(12)
    run2 = p.add_run(content)
    run2.font.size = Pt(12)

def add_page_break():
    run = doc.add_paragraph().add_run()
    run.add_break(WD_BREAK.PAGE)

# -------- SECTION A --------
# A1
add_heading("A1. Cover: title and promise")
add_block("Purpose:", "Introduce the guide and set a clear promise for what you will learn.")
add_block("What investors expect:", "A clean title and a single message about the outcome of using this guide.")
add_block("Gather this first:", "Your studio or working title, a single in-engine image if you have one, and today’s date.")
add_block("Build it in your slide tool:", "Choose a Title layout. Title size 44–52 pt. Subtitle 26–30 pt with date and version. Place one in-engine image on the right or centre. Keep background plain. Add a small caption bottom right with file name and version.")
add_block("Copy-ready text:", "Title: Mobile Game Pitch Decks: A Beginner’s Guide to Seed, Series A, and Due Diligence. Body: Learn how to plan, build, and quality check investor-ready decks. Follow the step-by-step cards, then export this guide to Word or PDF for reference.")
add_block("Figure:", "[Figure: In-engine screenshot. Labels: build number if available, capture date.]")
add_block("Callouts:", "[Pro tip Keep this guide open on one screen while building your own deck on another.] [Caution Do not use concept art in place of real gameplay once you have a build.]")
add_block("QA checks:", "Title readable at arm’s length. Date and version visible. Image is real gameplay or a neutral placeholder.")
add_block("Common mistakes:", "Busy backgrounds, tiny subtitles, undated files.")
add_block("Footnote:", "File name example: Pitch_Guide_v1.0_2025-11-11.docx.")
add_page_break()

# A2
add_heading("A2. How to use this guide")
add_block("Purpose:", "Show you how to learn and build, even if this is your first deck.")
add_block("What investors expect:", "A short, focused pitch with clean visuals and dated facts.")
add_block("Gather this first:", "A blank slide file, your brand colours and fonts, a folder for charts and data, and a simple file name pattern like Game_Pitch_Seed_v1.0_2025-11-11.pptx.")
add_block("Build it in your slide tool:", "Make a two-column slide. Left column explains the 10-block card structure. Right column shows the working loop: Read a card, Gather assets, Build the slide, Add a caption and one-line takeaway, Run the QA checks, Tick the checklist. Keep Title ≥ 40 pt, Body ≥ 24 pt.")
add_block("Copy-ready text:", "Title: How to work with these cards. Body: Read one card, gather what it asks for, then build the slide step by step. Write a one-line takeaway under each figure, add a dated caption, run the QA checks, then move on.")
add_block("Figure:", "[Figure: Simple flow diagram labelled Read → Gather → Build → Caption → QA → Tick.]")
add_block("Callouts:", "[Pro tip Keep this guide open while you build your deck in a second window.] [Caution Do not skip captions. Captions carry dates, methods, and definitions.]")
add_block("QA checks:", "One clear flow diagram. Font sizes as specified. A caption reminder included.")
add_block("Common mistakes:", "Trying to build many slides at once, skipping dates, mixing platforms in one chart.")
add_block("Footnote:", "File hygiene and versioning appear at the end of the guide.")
add_page_break()

# A3
add_heading("A3. Overview map: Seed vs Series A vs Due Diligence")
add_block("Purpose:", "Explain what changes between Seed, Series A, and the diligence pack.")
add_block("What investors expect:", "Seed shows promise and a plan. Series A shows proof that scales. Diligence proves your claims with organised files.")
add_block("Gather this first:", "Your stage, your next two quarters of goals, and a list of files you already have.")
add_block("Build it in your slide tool:", "Create a three-column table: Seed, Series A, Due Diligence. For each, list the core slide themes and the bar to clear. Add a small note that the pitch stays short and the deep files sit in the diligence folder.")
add_block("Copy-ready text:", "Title: What changes at each stage. Body: Seed focuses on timing, loop clarity, plan, and the ask. Series A upgrades each Seed topic with live metrics, cohorts, and unit economics. Diligence is a document index that proves every important claim.")
add_block("Figure:", "[Figure: Three-column table of stage differences.]")
add_block("Callouts:", "[Pro tip Keep the pitch under 20 slides at Seed and Series A. Put detail in the appendix and diligence.] [Caution Never blend platforms or channels in metrics.]")
add_block("QA checks:", "Stage differences are concrete. The pitch vs diligence split is stated.")
add_block("Common mistakes:", "Stuffing diligence pages into the pitch, vague “we will” claims without gates.")
add_block("Footnote:", "A full diligence index is included later in Section D.")
add_page_break()

# Build a single Word document that compiles the full
# "Mobile Game Pitch Deck Guide (Novice, 2025)" with Sections A–H.
#
# This file includes the previously supplied A1–A3 and B1–B17,
# plus the complete Series A section (C1–C17) and Sections D–H,
# all written in the same novice, explanatory style and 10-block blueprint.
#
# A page break is inserted after every card.
from docx import Document
from docx.shared import Pt
from docx.enum.text import WD_BREAK

doc = Document()

def add_heading(text, size=18):
    p = doc.add_paragraph()
    run = p.add_run(text)
    run.bold = True
    run.font.size = Pt(size)

def add_block(label, content):
    p = doc.add_paragraph()
    run = p.add_run(f"{label} ")
    run.bold = True
    run.font.size = Pt(12)
    p.add_run(content).font.size = Pt(12)

def add_page_break():
    doc.add_paragraph().add_run().add_break(WD_BREAK.PAGE)

def add_card(title, blocks):
    add_heading(title)
    for k, v in blocks.items():
        add_block(f"{k}:", v)
    add_page_break()

[USER]
Okay, this chat is really bogging down. Will you write me a detailed prompt to transition all of the details of this conversation in this chat over to a new chat window?

[ASSISTANT]
Here’s a paste-ready prompt you can drop into a brand-new chat to carry everything over. It sets roles, tone, scope, structure, and quality bars so the next session can continue without losing detail.

```
MODE: Novice Tutorial Build

Precedence
- Ignore any prior account or project instructions that conflict with THIS brief. Follow THIS brief first.
- Ask clarifying questions when information is missing or could change output quality. Keep questions short and outcome-changing only.

Roles
- You are an award-winning guide writer and teaching designer.
- You are also a coaching panel made up of a startup mobile game CEO, CTO, CPO, CFO, UA lead, and two investors. Use these roles to sanity-check content and explain the “why.”

Objective
- Produce a detailed, beginner-friendly teaching guide that shows how to build mobile game pitch decks for Seed, Series A, and a Due Diligence pack.
- Preserve full detail. Teach step by step. Explain concepts in plain British English.
- Tone is friendly and explanatory, not terse. No em dash. No icons.

What this is and how to use it
- This is a how-to guide, not a pitch deck. It teaches a first-time founder how to build a high quality investor deck from a blank file.
- Every page in the guide maps to one slide the founder will build. Each page explains: goal, what investors expect, what to gather, how to build, what to write, what to chart, pitfalls to avoid, and how to check quality.
- Output as a single document that can be exported to Word or PDF.

Global formatting for THIS guide
- Card-by-card. One card equals one page.
- Insert a line with exactly three hyphens between cards: ---
- Target 45 to 60 cards total.
- Each card 140 to 220 words unless it is a title card or glossary entry.
- British English. Friendly coaching voice. Clear sentences. No em dash, no icons.

Platform and data assumptions for THIS guide
- Do not lock the guide to a single platform. Teach platform-agnostic principles, then include a short annex with platform-specific KPI gates for Telegram Mini Apps, Poki, iOS, Android, and Steam.
- The guide may use small example datasets. Where a studio lacks data, teach with tiny illustrative CSV snippets the reader can replace later.
- Show dual currency everywhere numbers appear: GBP and USD.
- The guide will not embed gameplay video. Provide capture scripts and captions that a founder can use in their pitch decks.

Card blueprint for EVERY card (use this exact order and labels)
1) Purpose – one plain sentence that states the goal of the slide the reader will build.
2) What investors expect – one sentence that names the bar to clear.
3) Gather this first – concrete assets or data and where to get them.
4) Build it in your slide tool – numbered, click-by-click steps from a blank slide to a finished slide in PowerPoint, Keynote, or Google Slides. Name the layout, where to place text and figures, and font sizes: Title ≥ 40 pt, Body ≥ 24 pt, Labels ≥ 16 pt.
5) Copy-ready text – final slide title and two body sentences the reader can paste verbatim.
6) Figure – a [Figure: …] placeholder plus exact labels and units. If a chart is involved, include a tiny CSV snippet and the axis labels to use.
7) Callouts – add one to three where helpful:
   [Callout: Pro tip …]  [Callout: Caution …]  [Callout: Critical to consider …]  [Callout: Deep Dive …]
8) QA checks – four to six pass criteria a reviewer can tick.
9) Common mistakes – three bullets to avoid.
10) Footnote – add [Footnote: source, date, method note] for any claim or number.

Global visual rules for slides the reader will build
- One primary visual per slide. Always label axes, units, and dates. Use high contrast. Left-align text.
- Tables in the core deck should be six rows by four columns or fewer; larger tables move to an appendix slide.
- Figures that involve money must show GBP and USD side by side.

Section flow for the guide
A) Front matter – 3 cards
   1. Cover: title and promise with a single in-engine image or a 5 to 10 second loop
   2. How to use this guide – what each section teaches and how to follow along
   3. Overview map – Seed vs Series A vs Due Diligence and how they differ
---
B) Seed deck tutorial – minimum 15 cards
   1. Title and promise
   2. Why now – timing drivers
   3. Market size and reachable SOM – bottom-up example with mini CSV
   4. Player problem and desire – quotes or clip guidance
   5. Fifteen-second hook – capture spec and captioning
   6. Core loop and two systems – diagram steps
   7. Distribution advantage – table plus channel rationale
   8. Monetisation plan – SKU ladder and first purchase path
   9. Early metrics OR LTV model with sensitivity – mini dataset and formula
   10. Go-to-market for 180 days – experiment calendar with gates
   11. Competitive landscape and your wedge – simple 2 by 2 or table
   12. Team fit – coverage map and two-line bios
   13. Milestones and KPI gates – timeline with exits
   14. Financial snapshot – burn line and unit targets
   15. The ask – round, use of proceeds, data room link
   + Add “Checklist: Seed readiness” and “Self-test: move to Series A”
---
C) Series A deck tutorial – minimum 15 cards
   Mirror Seed, but upgrade each topic with live data:
   1. Title with current state – installs, MAU, revenue run rate
   2. Progress since Seed – experiment wins and kills
   3. Product deep dive – live-ops cadence and economy stability
   4. Traction overview – D1, D7, D30, payer rate, ARPDAU, refunds
   5. Cohorts and retention – by platform, no blends, mini CSV
   6. Monetisation deep dive – SKU ladder, first purchase funnel, ad stack
   7. Unit economics – LTV by cohort and channel, CAC, payback with bands
   8. Growth engine – channel mix, creative taxonomy, creator programmes
   9. Tech and operations – pipeline, analytics, compliance
   10. Scale roadmap – markets, content cadence, infra
   11. Team for scale – hires and open roles mapped to risks
   12. Financials – drivers, burn, runway, scenarios
   13. EU distribution and fees impact – margin bridge example
   14. Risks and mitigations – owners and next checkpoints
   15. The ask – round size, tranche plan, links
   + Add “Checklist: Series A readiness” and “Self-test: proceed to diligence”
---
D) Due Diligence overview – 10 to 18 cards
   Executive summary with deep links; legal and company structure; cap table and options; IP and contractor assignments; product docs and roadmap index; live-ops calendars and release process; data schema and event dictionary; cohort exports and method notes; attribution method per platform; UA logs and creative taxonomy with results; community and creator programmes; financial history and forecasts; budget by function and vendor schedules; UA spend logs and reconciliation; technology and security; compliance posture; risk register with owners; appendix index. Each card includes a short “how to read this” note and a Footnote where policy appears.
---
E) Tutorials and worked examples – 6 to 10 cards
   LTV and payback step-by-step; cohort chart build; EU fee margin bridge with a simple waterfall; experiment design template; “build this slide” recipes for loop, store ladder, and timeline; evidence table template with example entries.
---
F) Platform annex with KPI gates – 5 cards
   One card each for Telegram Mini Apps, Poki, iOS, Android, Steam. For each: entry points, test windows, KPI gates, compliance notes, and an example experiment.
---
G) Glossary – 2 to 4 cards
   Define TAM, SAM, SOM, D1, D7, D30, ARPDAU, ARPPU, LTV, CAC, payback, FTUE, UA, CPI, ROAS, SKAN, AdAttributionKit, Android Privacy Sandbox.
---
H) Final checklists and rubrics – 2 to 3 cards
   One-minute skim, readability and completeness; acceptance rubric 0 to 5 with examples of what earns a 5; versioning and change log instructions.

Data and figures rules
- Any chart card must include a mini CSV and the exact axis labels, units, and date range. Show one worked calculation where helpful.
- Add a caption that names the method or attribution used. Keep platforms and channels separate. Never blend cohorts.

Quality bars to enforce on every card
- One clear claim per slide. A visible takeaway line under the figure.
- Dates on every metric and figure. Net figures where money appears, with GBP and USD side by side.
- Methods in captions. Accessibility: chart labels readable at 100 percent zoom; contrast suitable for WCAG AA.
- Include QA checks and Common mistakes on every card.

Known answers to the usual clarifications
1) Platforms in scope: teach platform-agnostic in the body and provide a platform annex for Telegram Mini Apps, Poki, iOS, Android, and Steam.
2) Data availability: the guide is not a pitch. Use tiny illustrative datasets and show how to replace them with real data later.
3) Brand fonts and colours: none specified. Use neutral defaults and teach where to apply brand later.
4) Currency: always show both GBP and USD.
5) Video: do not embed gameplay video in the guide. Provide a 15-second capture script and caption that the founder can use in their pitch deck.

Start now
- Rebuild or reprint the complete guide A to H using the blueprint above. Keep the tone explanatory and friendly. Do not summarise away detail. If token limits force a split, continue where you left off.
